
A high-tech battle over immigrants; Firms seek to import more professionals
The San Diego Union-Tribune
June 8, 1998 Monday


Copyright 1998 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: NEWS; Pg. A-1
Length: 2202 words
Byline: Marcus Stern, COPLEY NEWS SERVICE
Dateline: WASHINGTON 
Body


WASHINGTON -- The biggest immigration battle of the year is coming to a head this month, but the focus isn't on fences, farm workers or food stamps. Instead, it's on computer programs and the people who write them. For the past year, American high-tech companies have been working with Congress to expand an 8-year-old program that brings in 65,000 foreign professionals each year.
The companies want the limit raised to 115,000. Because the visas are good for up to six years, the increase would mean that -- under this program alone -- as many as 690,000 foreign workers eventually could be employed in the United States at any given time. The lobbying has continued at a time when some of these companies have laid off American workers or imposed hiring freezes.
Last month, Intel, one of the driving forces behind the program's expansion, said it would cut 3,000 positions because of slow computer sales. One crucial question is at the center of the upcoming congressional vote: Should lawmakers add protections to help American programmers and engineers who compete for these coveted jobs? Most of the foreign workers come from India and China, where they likely earned about $2,500 a year.
In the United States, they stand to make almost 15 times that amount. U.S. employers say safeguards aren't needed.
They say they hire foreign workers only when they can't find skilled Americans to do the job. "If we had rampant problems with the program, I probably wouldn't even be proposing an increase," said Sen. Spencer Abraham, R-Mich., chairman of the Senate immigration subcommittee and the high-tech industry's champion on this issue. "We don't have problems with the program, because we have very responsible employers who are truly playing by the rules." But the rules in this high-stakes game are extraordinarily loose: [] Some of the top users of the program, which requires an H-1B visa, are foreign-owned firms that don't consider hiring American engineers or programmers.
They recruit only from abroad. [] Current safeguards, designed to make sure employers can't bring in H-1B workers at low wages, don't work because "prevailing wage" figures are often misleading. [] Labor Department officials say their hands are tied and their eyes blindfolded under the current regulations.
They've asked Congress for either a meaningful oversight role or no role at all. [] The Immigration and Naturalization Service has so completely abdicated its H-1B responsibilities that it doesn't bother to check if the foreign workers go home when their visas expire. [] Many of the most active H-1B importers are temporary staffing agencies that recruit foreign workers simply to lease them to U.S. companies.
The U.S. companies like the arrangement, because they can hire for specific projects and then let workers go when the projects are over.
This lowers their overall labor costs. High-tech companies were delighted last month when the Senate passed a bill that would increase the number of guest workers without tightening the regulations. But they reacted angrily to the bill that's now moving through the House. It would require employers to attest that they at least tried to find American workers, and that they hadn't laid off anyone to make room for an H-1B worker. The regulation would add two checkoff boxes to the current one-page Labor Department form.
But some high-tech employers say that is too much.
They'd scrap the H-1B increase entirely, rather than put up with new regulations. The final shape of the law that Congress passes in the coming weeks could determine the value of tomorrow's U.S. high-tech jobs. If companies continue their growing reliance on temporary foreign workers, will these prized jobs of the 1990s retain their luster in the 21st century? Or will wages and working conditions erode as the high-tech work force fills with foreign programmers willing to work longer hours for less money? [] [] [] Saju James sits nattily attired in a double-breasted blazer in the conference room of Tata Consultancy Services in the Washington suburb of Rockville, Md. Tata is an international technology firm based in India and one of the biggest users of the H-1B program.
With its corporate cousin, Tata Infotech, it was the second-biggest recipient of H-1B visas last year at 1,128. Tata Consultancy, the larger of the cousins, has about 1,500 people working in the United States.
The programmers are paid by Tata, but they actually work under contract for some of America's biggest and best-known technology firms. James' job is to obtain their H-1B visas. Upon request, he brings out files on 13 Tata employees working in San Diego on a project with UCSD. The H-1B law requires employers to make the files available for public inspection. All 13 were top graduates of India's premier technical institutes, he said, and each had 18 months of specialized on-the-job training in India before coming to the United States. The group at UCSD is working on a multimedia project that requires many of today's "hot skills."
They are precisely the kinds of programmers in demand in today's labor market. Tata pays them an average of $35,300 a year. Although entry-level salaries in the high-tech industry fluctuate, that figure appears to be low. At a Senate hearing earlier this year, a CEO said his company gave a new car to one engineering graduate as a signing bonus.
A University of Michigan dean told of a prevailing wage of $66,851 for someone with an applications programmer degree. Yet the Tata engineers, who have equivalent degrees, make only about half that much. The H-1B program's loose regulations, which the computer industry helped shape, make the wide range in salaries possible. Employers are required to pay their H-1B workers at least the "prevailing wage," a figure drawn from salary surveys of local businesses. But employers are allowed to pick and choose from a wide range of salary surveys, including some that are outdated or flawed.
Naturally, they tend to pick the lowest prevailing wage available. The prevailing wage listed for the Tata employees at UCSD is $33,370. The source is the Economic Research Institute. An official with the institute freely admitted that its survey shows a higher prevailing wage -- $37,700. But, he said, a government survey lists a prevailing wage of $33,176. The rate is artificially low, he said, because it is based on 2-year-old data and lumps professionals with paraprofessionals. Tata's James concedes that $35,300 may seem low in today's hot job market. Especially when you're talking about people as highly skilled as the Tata engineers. But by Indian standards, $35,300 is a phenomenal salary, he said.
At home, these programmers would make about $2,500 a year. "With the money they save here, they can go back to India and build a house," he said. As James sits surrounded by bare walls in Tata's sparsely furnished office in Rockville, he wonders aloud how the main safeguards in the House bill would affect Tata. The one requiring Tata to attest that it hadn't laid off any American workers would be no problem.
Tata doesn't have any American programmers or engineers to lay off.
It simply doesn't hire American. But the requirement that companies recruit American workers could spell trouble. All of Tata's recruiting is done in India. Of the 1,500 Tata employees working in the United States, James can think of only one, a vice president, who is American. [] [] [] Tata Consultancy isn't the only H-1B user without American engineers. Tata Infotech, its corporate cousin, has about 500 employees in the United States, also under contract with U.S. firms.
About 95 percent of them are here on H-1Bs, a company official said.
Most of the rest are here on L-1 visas, which are temporary visas for support staff. Another top user of H-1Bs, Mastech Systems of Oakdale, Pa., reports that the overwhelming majority of its engineers and programmers, also on contract to U.S. technology firms, are in the United States on temporary work visas. Mastech was the single-largest H-1B user last year, with 1,689. Rep. Bruce Morrison, a Connecticut Democrat who was chairman of the House immigration subcommittee when the H-1B program was created in 1990 and takes fatherly pride in the program, says the practices of companies like Tata Consultancy, Tata Infotech and Mastech raise questions beyond immigration law -- questions of hiring discrimination. "This isn't an H-1B problem, it's a Title VII problem," he said. [] [] [] Sen. Abraham and industry representatives are fond of saying the H-1B program is "totally transparent."
Anybody can go to a workplace and see how much an H-1B worker earns.
Theoretically, this ensures they're not paid less than other workers, thereby undermining the U.S. wage structure. That may be the law, but it's not how it works. When Qualcomm's Daniel L. Sullivan, senior vice president for human resources, appeared before the House immigration subcommittee to push for an expanded H-1B program, he also said that the program was well-regulated and that the proposed employee protections were unnecessary. San Diego-based Qualcomm has 10,600 employees in the United States.
Of those, 572 -- about 5.5 percent of the company's work force -- are on temporary work visas. "Current regulations require Qualcomm and every other business to pay H-1B employees at the same rate we pay nonimmigrant personnel in the same job categories," Sullivan said, adding that he has seen "no evidence that employers are attempting to evade the prevailing wage regulation." Several weeks later, in an office at Qualcomm's headquarters, a company administrator opened an accordion file packed with information on all the company's H-1B employees. Last year, the telecommunications company brought in 122 H-1B workers.
For many, the listed prevailing wage was $33,200. The source was the Radford Survey. Steve Radford is head of the Radford Division of Aon Consulting.
He has been surveying high-tech compensation practices for 23 years.
He said the "effective" prevailing wage range for an entry-level programmer in San Diego is between $38,000 and $45,000. Qualcomm's Bill Bold, who has lobbied in Washington to expand the H-1B program, said Qualcomm pays its foreign professionals more than the prevailing wage listed on the Labor Department forms stuffed into the accordion file.
But he wouldn't say how much. He said the company "as a rule" doesn't pay less than $42,000 for entry-level programmers like the ones hired under the H-1B program and often offers a signing bonus and company stock. But he refused to disclose the actual wages of the H-1B workers.
Initially, company officials had promised to, but after weeks of delays decided against it. Without knowing the actual wages, it is impossible to determine whether Qualcomm is paying foreign workers the same as its other entry-level programmers, or if the workers are lumped at the bottom of the company's salary ladder. [] [] [] Sen. Abraham may have confidence in the way the H-1B program is being run, but the officials who run it don't. Labor Department officials complain that regulations force them to rubber-stamp H-1B applications. They can reject the applications only when they aren't completely filled out and signed, or if the forms contain information that's clearly incorrect.
Otherwise, approval comes within seven days. At the INS, spokesman Russ Bergeron said his agency's only role in the H-1B program is to count the number of visas approved and make sure it doesn't exceed the legal limit. Actually, the agency must approve each H-1B visa.
But the process is so perfunctory that it's easy to see how Bergeron could overlook it.
All the INS has to do is make sure the applicant's occupation qualifies under the program. Although employers must fill out a three-page form for the INS, only a small portion of the information is entered into a database.
Most of it remains on the application form, stored in one of four INS service centers nationwide. The agency keeps track of how many visas are approved, but not of how many are actually issued.
Nor does it keep track of when temporary workers arrive in the country, when they are supposed to leave, where they will be working, or what they will be doing while they're here. And who makes sure the workers eventually go home? "That's the responsibility of the employers," Bergeron said. About the program [] The H-1B program was created by Congress in 1990 to provide U.S. employers each year with access to up to 65,000 foreign professionals in certain specialty occupations.
The idea was to help American employers stay competitive in the global economy. [] Most of these workers are computer programmers, engineers, physical therapists, accountants and teachers.
Foreign fashion models, chefs and athletes also use the visas.
In recent years, the program has become increasingly dominated by the high-tech industry. [] The H-1B is good for three years and is renewable once, making it valid for a total of six years.
But H-1B holders must remain with the employer who obtained it for them or the visa becomes invalid.
For that reason, critics have said the visa leaves the workers effectively indentured to their employers.

Bill to hire foreigners runs into turbulence; White House dislikes compromise measure
The San Diego Union-Tribune
July 31, 1998 Friday


Copyright 1998 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: NEWS; Pg. A-1
Length: 718 words
Byline: Marcus Stern, COPLEY NEWS SERVICE
Dateline: WASHINGTON 
Body


WASHINGTON -- Lobbyists for the high-tech industry began yesterday with victory at hand in their long quest for congressional approval to hire more foreign workers.
But by day's end, they were in disarray and facing possible defeat. Key House and Senate Republicans had reached a final agreement yesterday morning shortly after midnight to increase the flow of foreign professionals by as many as 190,000 over the next six years. Votes were promptly scheduled in the House and Senate by GOP leaders and the bill was to be laid on President Clinton's desk over the weekend. But industry's apparent victory unraveled during the course of the hot afternoon.
The key moment came when the White House, after reading the 34-page compromise, issued a veto threat late yesterday that was even stronger than one it had issued on July 16. "Unfortunately, this Republican agreement fails to provide adequate training and protections for U.S. workers," said White House spokesman Jake Siewert.
"In its current form, the president would have no choice but to veto it.
We are working on proposed revisions to the Republicans' bill and would like to address the skills shortage before Congress leaves on recess." But that seemed increasingly unlikely as the day wore on. Sen. Edward M. Kennedy, D-Mass., a longtime opponent of the program, which allows 65,000 new foreign professionals to enter the country each year and stay for up to six years, announced plans to block debate on the bill in the Senate.
The bill would greatly expand the H-1B program, so-named for the visa the foreign workers are issued. Meanwhile, in the House, Rep. Elton Gallegly, R-Simi Valley, a member of the Judiciary Committee, began urging other Republicans to oppose the GOP compromise.
Sources said he wanted tighter restrictions on the program.
He could not be reached for comment late yesterday. But by the end of the day, the House Republican leadership, which had hoped to move the GOP compromise quickly to the president's desk for his signature, had cancelled a vote scheduled for today on the compromise legislation. The Senate is planning to recess this weekend.
Even if the House reschedules the vote for next week, the Senate would not be around to approve the bill.
Therefore, it seemed likely late yesterday that final action would be put off until Congress returns from its break in early September. Some warned that the window of opportunity might be closed by then. "How many people want to vote for 190,000 new foreign workers two months before an election?" said a key Republican staff member. Companies lobbying for the bill, including Qualcomm, San Diego's wireless communications giant, had been forced to stop hiring under the H-1B program May 11 because the annual allotment of 65,000 visas for all U.S. employers had been used.
The high-tech companies say they must hire foreign workers because there are not enough skilled Americans to fill the available jobs -- a claim critics dispute. The agreement reached yesterday would raise the cap on the number of new H-1B workers to 85,000 this fiscal year, which ends Sept. 30; 95,000 in 1999; 105,000 in 2000; and 115,000 in fiscal years 2001 and 2002. The annual cap then would return to the current level of 65,000. The GOP compromise would require some -- but not all -- companies using the H-1B program to affirm that they had taken reasonable steps to recruit American workers before hiring a foreign worker. They also would have to report to the government that they had not laid off an American worker with similar skills in the same geographic area 90 days before or after applying for the H-1B worker. But critics of the bill yesterday said fewer than 5 percent of the companies using the H-1B program would be forced to abide by the new rules. "This is a loophole for big companies to hire cheap indentured labor," said Rep. Ron Klink, D-Pa., an opponent. The compromise also would require companies to pay a $250 fee for each H-1B visa it receives for a foreign worker.
The money would go into two funds for high-tech training and education.
White House spokesman Siewert said the money raised by the fee would be insufficient. American Business for Legal Immigration, an industry group, had called the compromise "fair and flexible" and urged Congress to approve it quickly.

What is America doing to its free labor market?
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 24, 1998 Thursday


Copyright 1998 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. B-9:1,3,5,6,7,8; B-11:2
Length: 1042 words
Byline: John R. Reinert, REINERT is president of The Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers, USA.
Body


Stephen has a house near Silicon Valley, a wife and two children -- one of whom requires expensive medical treatment and the full-time care of his mother. Although he possesses marketable skills like C++, PLC and Visual Basic, the 48-year-old engineer and computer programmer hasn't been able to land a permanent job since he was replaced last year by a lower-paid foreign worker at a well-known electronics firm.
To pay the bills, he works on a temporary contract training his replacement. Sree is an engineer on an H-1B skilled-employment visa at a major U.S. manufacturer.
He has been refused a raise for the past two years and says that he could work elsewhere for 40 percent more than he is making now -- except that he can't work for another employer according to the terms of his visa.
If his green card comes through, he says, his employer won't be upset to see him leave because there will likely be another H-1B visa holder to take his place. Stephen and Sree are among the forgotten ones in the current debate over expanding the H-1B guest-worker program for temporary foreign professionals.
The House of Representatives is scheduled to vote sometime later this week on H.R. 3736, a bill that would increase by nearly 200,000 the number of high-tech braceros that U.S. companies could sponsor during the next four years. Some special-interest groups, continuing to cite a study discredited by the General Accounting Office, argue that expanding the H-1B program is the only solution to an acute shortage of high-tech workers.
Yet the usual signs of a labor shortage in this area are absent.
Wage increases of engineers and information-technology workers have tracked closely with those of other professional groups, while hiring rates in these fields are quite low -- commonly 2 percent to 4 percent of applicants at major companies.
At the same time, reports of high demand have spurred a 40 percent increase in computer-science majors. The real shortage today is of recently trained high-tech workers who will work long hours at entry-level salaries.
With a limited pool of recent grads available, companies look to the H-1B program to provide a large pool of inexpensive foreign labor.
Unlike permanent immigrants who enjoy the same workplace rights as other American professionals, these workers are virtually indentured to their employers, subject to deportation if they lose their corporate sponsorship.
As a result, they are often hired at lower salaries and don't enjoy the same opportunities for advancement as their colleagues.
Their substantial presence in the work force also depresses wages and working conditions for all technical professionals. Expanding the H-1B program throws a monkey wrench into the free-market system.
Instead of letting the natural mechanism of wage rates regulate the size of the labor force, the federal government would increase a pool of artificially low-priced labor that allows industry to meet demand without raising wages.
As with other attempts by Congress to tamper with the basic mechanics of free markets, this one has several untoward consequences. First, by making it cheaper to hire H-1B workers than to hire or retrain U.S. workers, Congress gives industry a strong incentive to shun qualified older workers like Stephen who are unable to work as cheaply.
According to a recent study by the National Science Foundation, only 19 percent of computer-science graduates are working in their field just 20 years after graduation.
And of those who do try to stay?
The unemployment rate last year for computer programmers over 50 years of age was a whopping 17 percent, according to Computerworld magazine.
Meanwhile, many workers who could be retrained for jobs in hot, new technologies are overlooked because it is faster and cheaper to hire H-1B workers. Second, by indenturing foreign workers to their employers, the government creates an environment of exploitation of temporary foreign professionals like Sree. Without the essential tools to negotiate a fair market value for labor, H-1B workers very often can't obtain better salaries, raises and promotions. Third, the federal government never succeeds in balancing labor supply and demand.
The H-1B program began in 1991 amid dire predictions by industry of a looming engineers shortage.
But when tens of thousands of temporary high-tech workers began to hit the shores, labor demand suddenly contracted due to recession, corporate restructuring and massive defense cutbacks.
The result was the highest engineering unemployment rate in history and declining wages by 1994. Now Congress is considering a massive increase in the same program just as the cycle is beginning to turn down again, with more than 200,000 layoffs in the high-tech sector during the past several months and a near tripling of the engineering unemployment rate in the last quarter. Finally, by relying on temporary high-tech guest-workers, the United States risks the gradual erosion of its vital technical infrastructure.
Each additional guest-worker is another lost opportunity to develop the permanent U.S. technical work force, both native and foreign-born.
The cumulative effect of hundreds of thousands of these missed investments is already being felt.
U.S. real engineering wages are no higher than they were 10 years ago, even as profits have soared in the high-tech sector. Since 1992, the number of permanent admissions of foreign engineers and computer scientists has declined by more than 20 percent, though temporary admissions have nearly doubled. This summer at a Cato Institute forum on H-1B legislation, the U.S. Chamber of Commerce's Bruce Josten said that part of the American advantage is the "freedom to move people."
We disagree.
The true American advantage is the freedom of people to move -- to better careers, to better pay, to better opportunities that enable them to improve their lives even as they help improve the U.S. standard of living.
It is that capacity to energize the individual that is the genius of the American free-enterprise system -- and why our high-tech industry is the envy of the world.
Increased reliance on indentured guest-workers robs U.S. workers of rewarding, life-long careers and deprives guest-workers of basic rights in the workplace.

U.S. aims to expand high-tech visa plan; Skilled foreigners filling acute needs
The San Diego Union-Tribune
March 10, 2000 Friday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS; Pg. C-1
Length: 743 words
Byline: Joe Cantlupe, COPLEY NEWS SERVICE
Dateline: WASHINGTON 
Body


WASHINGTON -- Like an old computer running out of memory, the U.S. visa program that allows high-tech foreign workers into this country once again is rapidly reaching its limit. The Clinton administration and Congress are getting ready to upgrade the system. But there appears to be sharp disagreement about exactly how that should be done. The Senate Judiciary Committee took a first step yesterday, approving a bill that would sharply boost the number of high-tech foreign workers allowed into the United States each year. The increase has been sought by San Diego's Chamber of Commerce, the wireless communication giant, Qualcomm, and other high-tech businesses, to address what they view as severe labor shortages. Two years ago, Congress raised the visa ceiling from 65,000 to 115,000 under the law that calls for the allotment to drop to 107,500 in 2001 and back to 65,000 thereafter. Since then, the high-tech industry has hungered for more.
It says the existing visa quota has hamstrung a burgeoning industry that faces a shortage of highly skilled workers in this country.
The so-called H-1B visas allow foreigners to work in the United States for three years.
The visas can be renewed once, for a total of six years. Industry representatives hailed the Senate panel's 15-3 vote to approve a measure that would dramatically raise the cap. Under the bill sponsored by Sens. Orrin Hatch, R-Utah, Spencer Abraham, R-Mich., Dianne Feinstein, D-Calif., and others, the quota would be raised to 195,000 H-1B visas each year for the next three years. The Senate panel also approved a Feinstein amendment that would provide grants and scholarships for science and technology programs. The decision "sends a strong message about the need to address this critical problem," said Sandra Boyd, chairman of the American Business for Legal Immigration, a group of more than 200 companies and associations. "U.S. employers must have access to an adequate supply of qualified workers in this country if we are to continue to grow the economy," Boyd said. But the Clinton administration suggests the Senate panel's bill may be unacceptable to the president because it lacks a training provision for U.S. workers. "We are ready to support a reasonable increase in the number of H-1B visas as long as it reflects a balanced approach that protects and prepares the U.S. work force," White House spokesman Jake Siewart said. Some Democratic and Republican lawmakers favor a more limited cap increase, saying the labor shortages cited by the high-tech industry have not been proved.
They also said they want assurances that authorities crack down on fraud and provide enough protections for American workers. Other critics -- including those in organized labor and anti-immigration groups -- contend the high-tech industry simply wants to obtain cheap labor and hold down wages. But representatives of the high-tech industry in San Diego said this week the quotas cannot be raised quickly enough, and the need for skilled workers is significant. "We're happy with plans to increase the high-tech visas," said Diana Baldwin, a spokeswoman for Qualcomm, which has lobbied the government to increase the cap. Angela Villagrana, a spokeswoman for the San Diego Chamber of Commerce, added: "There is a shortage of skilled workers and our high-tech companies need to expand.
We realize it's the brain power of the future." Yesterday, Sen. Edward M. Kennedy, D-Mass. offered a narrower version of the Hatch bill, calling for a 145,000 ceiling.
Kennedy proposed a massive infusion of funding to train U.S. workers for high-tech jobs. The Kennedy effort was rejected. Earlier this month, Rep. Lamar Smith, D-Tex., chairman of the House immigration subcommittee, introduced a measure to raise the cap to 160,000 for the fiscal year 2000. But Smith said the increases would be contingent on efforts to reduce fraud within the visa program. High-tech industry officials said Smith's bill would stifle the economy. More than half the H-1B visas are obtained by citizens of India or China. The volume of H-1B visa applications was so great last year the INS "effectively stopped taking applications" June 15, agency spokeswoman Eylene Schmidt said.
In 1999, the agency mistakenly issued 20,000 H-1B visas beyond the quota, officials have said. This year, the agency also has issued visas at a pace in which the existing 115,000 cap will be reached quickly, according to the high-tech industry.

U.S. aims to expand high-tech visa plan; Skilled foreigners filling acute needs
The San Diego Union-Tribune
March 10, 2000, Friday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. C-1; STATISTICS.
Length: 741 words
Byline: Joe Cantlupe; COPLEY NEWS SERVICE
Body

WASHINGTON -- Like an old computer running out of memory, the U.S. visa program that allows high-tech foreign workers into this country once again is rapidly reaching its limit.
The Clinton administration and Congress are getting ready to upgrade the system.
But there appears to be sharp disagreement about exactly how that should be done.
The Senate Judiciary Committee took a first step yesterday, approving a bill that would sharply boost the number of high-tech foreign workers allowed into the United States each year.
The increase has been sought by San Diego's Chamber of Commerce, the wireless communication giant, Qualcomm, and other high-tech businesses, to address what they view as severe labor shortages.
Two years ago, Congress raised the visa ceiling from 65,000 to 115,000 under the law that calls for the allotment to drop to 107,500 in 2001 and back to 65,000 thereafter.
Since then, the high-tech industry has hungered for more.  It says the existing visa quota has hamstrung a burgeoning industry that faces a shortage of highly skilled workers in this country.  The so-called H-1B visas allow foreigners to work in the United States for three years.  The visas can be renewed once, for a total of six years.
Industry representatives hailed the Senate panel's 15-3 vote to approve a measure that would dramatically raise the cap.
Under the bill sponsored by Sens. Orrin Hatch, R-Utah, Spencer Abraham, R-Mich., Dianne Feinstein, D-Calif., and others, the quota would be raised to 195,000 H-1B visas each year for the next three years.
The Senate panel also approved a Feinstein amendment that would provide grants and scholarships for science and technology programs.
The decision "sends a strong message about the need to address this critical problem," said Sandra Boyd, chairman of the American Business for Legal Immigration, a group of more than 200 companies and associations.
"U.S. employers must have access to an adequate supply of qualified workers in this country if we are to continue to grow the economy," Boyd said.
But the Clinton administration suggests the Senate panel's bill may be unacceptable to the president because it lacks a training provision for U.S. workers.
"We are ready to support a reasonable increase in the number of H-1B visas as long as it reflects a balanced approach that protects and prepares the U.S. work force," White House spokesman Jake Siewart said.
Some Democratic and Republican lawmakers favor a more limited cap increase, saying the labor shortages cited by the high-tech industry have not been proved.  They also said they want assurances that authorities crack down on fraud and provide enough protections for American workers.
Other critics -- including those in organized labor and anti-immigration groups -- contend the high-tech industry simply wants to obtain cheap labor and hold down wages.
But representatives of the high-tech industry in San Diego said this week the quotas cannot be raised quickly enough, and the need for skilled workers is significant.
"We're happy with plans to increase the high-tech visas," said Diana Baldwin, a spokeswoman for Qualcomm, which has lobbied the government to increase the cap.
Angela Villagrana, a spokeswoman for the San Diego Chamber of Commerce, added: "There is a shortage of skilled workers and our high-tech companies need to expand.  We realize it's the brain power of the future."
Yesterday, Sen. Edward M. Kennedy, D-Mass. offered a narrower version of the Hatch bill, calling for a 145,000 ceiling.  Kennedy proposed a massive infusion of funding to train U.S. workers for high-tech jobs.
The Kennedy effort was rejected.
Earlier this month, Rep. Lamar Smith, D-Tex., chairman of the House immigration subcommittee, introduced a measure to raise the cap to 160,000 for the fiscal year 2000. But Smith said the increases would be contingent on efforts to reduce fraud within the visa program.
High-tech industry officials said Smith's bill would stifle the economy.
More than half the H-1B visas are obtained by citizens of India or China.
The volume of H-1B visa applications was so great last year the INS "effectively stopped taking applications" June 15, agency spokeswoman Eylene Schmidt said.  In 1999, the agency mistakenly issued 20,000 H-1B visas beyond the quota, officials have said.
This year, the agency also has issued visas at a pace in which the existing 115,000 cap will be reached quickly, according to the high-tech industry.

Study looks at foreigners in high-tech jobs; 420,000 here now; bills may hike total
The San Diego Union-Tribune
April 19, 2000, Wednesday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. C-2; STATISTICS.
Length: 489 words
Byline: Diane Lindquist; STAFF WRITER
Body

Foreigners employed temporarily in U.S. high-tech jobs currently number more than 420,000, according to a new study released this week.
Under pending U.S. legislation, the total could rise within two years to at least 710,000, the study says.
Because of caps on employment visas and country quotas, only a small portion of the workers -- as little as 15 percent -- will be able to gain permanent, legal U.S. residency.  The report was prepared by the Institute for the Study of International Migration at Georgetown University.
In the past, about half of those admitted under the special category, known as H-1B, have sought to remain legally in the United States after their visas expired at the end of six years.
Susan Martin, the institute's director, said raising the annual foreign guest worker ceiling without addressing the impediments to permanent U.S. residency is irresponsible.
"The system will be producing tens of thousands of potential visa overstayers yearly," she said.
At least four measures have been introduced in Congress to raise the H-1B visa program beyond its current 115,000 annual limit, said Allen Kay, spokesman for Rep. Lamar Smith, R-Texas, chairman of the House immigration subcommittee.
Last year, the Immigration and Naturalization Service exceeded the limit on the visas by nearly 20,000. Officials blamed the error on improper tallying of totals from the four centers that process the highly sought but controversial visas.
The H-1B temporary work visa is the principle mechanism through which highly skilled workers and professionals enter the United States.  Created in 1990, it since has become known as the high-tech visa because of companies' vigorous efforts to increase the cap.
Critics say the technology companies are turning to foreign programmers and engineers as a way to get cheaper workers.
B. Lindsay Lowell, the report's author, said another study indicates there is a gap in earnings between permanent and temporary workers, including H-1Bs who hold master's degrees or above, in the high-tech industry.
The push for substantially raising or even removing the ceiling on H-1B visas is coming from Greater San Diego Chamber of Commerce, Qualcomm and other high-tech companies across the United States.
Current proposals in Congress for new caps range from 195,000 to Rep. Smith's suggestion for an unlimited number, said Kay.
Whether legislators also will address the foreign guest workers' dilemma in gaining permanent status, he said, "would be wildly speculative."
"The issue here is not really the numbers but the qualifications of the individuals receiving H-1B status," said Kay.
Nevertheless, until the migration institute's study, Congress has debated changes in the numbers with no clear idea of the size of the population of temporary foreign workers that has accumulated over the years.  Nor were legislators able to grasp the future implications for those seeking to gain legal residency.

Imported know-how; Debate over foreign labor gives Congress a workout
The San Diego Union-Tribune
July 16, 2000, Sunday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. I-1
Length: 820 words
Byline: Dean Calbreath; STAFF WRITER
Body

Sanja Jha, who oversees development of chipsets and systems software at Qualcomm, bristles when he hears complaints that U.S. companies are using special visas to import low-paid talent.
Jha, who was born in India and raised in Scotland, says the foreign workers on his team make just as much as their U.S. counterparts.  He says the reason he hires foreign workers is that it is hard to find U.S. talent.
"Finding new people is the biggest pain in my life," he says.
The debate over imported foreign labor is a point of contention as Congress considers expanding the H-1B visa program, which allows U.S. firms to hire skilled workers from abroad.  The issue is whether to raise the annual allotment of H-1Bs from 115,000 to 200,000 per year.
The H-1B visa -- it's named for a section of immigration law -- allows employers to hire foreigners who hold master's degrees or doctorates for three-year stints, provided they are paid no lower than 5 percent less than the prevailing wage in their profession.
Critics, including the AFL-CIO and lobbying groups of engineers, contend that companies often pay foreigners far below the 5 percent allowed by law.  By hiring low-paid foreign workers, critics say, employers are keeping U.S. wages low.
"Employers are addicted to the H-1B," says Norm Matloff, a professor at the University of California Davis and one of the leading critics of the program.  "Even though the law requires that businesses hire H-1Bs at the same (pay) rate as U.S. workers, there are zillions of loopholes that allow them to pay lower rates."
Many high-tech executives, including Jha, say H-1B workers are needed because there is a shortage of skilled domestic employees.
"We've got three times as many openings as there are local applicants," says Tony Chartrand, who does the hiring at Ericsson's operations in San Diego.  "If the competency we need can be found down the street, that's great.  But if the competency comes from India, we can work with that too."
Chartrand says that in a way, he would prefer to hire domestic workers, since it takes a lot of time and money to hire a foreigner.  There's the expense of hiring an immigration lawyer and the aggravation of repeated delays in the application process.
Jha notes that each time he casts a net for employees, he typically extends offers to about 20-25 people, but only 12 to 15 accept.  "The acceptance rate used to be a lot higher, but San Diego has gotten so competitive that a lot of people are handling three or four offers," he says.
Given the intense competition, Jha says, it would be foolish for Qualcomm to offer job applicants a low-ball salary -- H-1B or not.
"We have to offer very competitive salaries if we're going to lure talent from such competitors as Motorola or Lucent," Jha says.  "Besides, we invest so much time and energy in the interviewing process that it would be a waste of money to have someone reject our offer because it's low."
Qualcomm's H-1B workers -- most of whom are software engineers -- make an average salary of about $66,532. That's about 5 percent ahead of the average salary for domestic software engineers, which is pegged at $63,199 by the U.S. Department of Labor.
Of the 212 H-1B applications reviewed by the Union-Tribune, only two workers made less than $40,000. Qualcomm, which has 7,000 local workers, has filed about 700 H-1B visa applications, although not all slots have been filled.
Similarly, of the 36 H-1B applications for Ericsson reviewed by the Union-Tribune, only one employee made less than $40,000, while three others made more than $125,000. Ericsson's operation in San Diego has 900 employees, and 175 of them are H-1B workers.
Yash Kharia is a H-1B worker.
In the mid-1990s, Kharia, who comes from a family of engineers in India, went to the India Institute of Technology, or IIT, paying tuition of just $22 per semester.
The main hurdle was the stringent entrance exam, since IIT accepts only 3 percent of applicants, compared to 11-18 percent at Ivy League schools in the United States.
After graduating, Kharia worked briefly at a short-lived Qualcomm operation in India.  When Qualcomm closed the plant in 1999, Kharia switched to Motorola.  But then his former boss at Qualcomm got in touch, saying he had landed a job at Ericsson in the United States and invited Kharia on board.
"All I had to do was fill out some forms that I got over the Internet and Ericsson did the rest," says Kharia, who says he is adequately compensated.
Not all H-1B employees are well-paid, though.
Marotz, a software firm in Jamul, typically pays its H-1Bs as little as $27,831 during their first six months in the United States.  Gradually, the employees -- most of them come from the former Soviet Union -- work their way to the $50,000 or $60,000 level.  But that can take more than a year.
Marotz appears to be an anomaly.  A review of H-1B filings shows that no other company in San Diego County pays that low.

1 CHART; Source: U.S. Department of Justice, Immigration and Naturalization Service. 1998-99 figures; H-1B Occupations

Company: QUALCOMM INC  (58%); QUALCOMM INC  (58%);    US DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE   (59%);  US DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE  (59%);  AFL-CIO  (55%);  UNIVERSITY OF CALIFORNIA   (54%);  UNIVERSITY OF CALIFORNIA  (54%)

Organization: US DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE   (59%);  US DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE  (59%);  AFL-CIO  (55%);  UNIVERSITY OF CALIFORNIA   (54%);  UNIVERSITY OF CALIFORNIA  (54%)

Ticker: QCOM (NASDAQ)  (58%)

Geographic: SAN DIEGO, CA, USA (94%); CALIFORNIA, USA (92%); INDIA (92%); UNITED STATES (79%); SCOTLAND (58%)

Company:  QUALCOMM INC (58%)

A need for foreign-born professionals
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 24, 2000 Sunday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. G-3
Length: 919 words
Byline: Jeanne Malitz, Malitz is a member of the San Diego World Trade Center and attorney at law with Morrison & Foerster LLP in San Diego.
Body


The H-1B program is a prompt, lawful way for U.S. employers to hire foreign-born professionals (such as engineers and computer programmers) on a temporary basis.
This program allows U.S. businesses to recruit and hire the best-qualified candidates from around the world and compete on a level playing field with foreign companies in such key industries as telecommunications, pharmaceuticals, biotechnology and education. The issue before Congress is whether the number of H-1B visas granted by the U.S. government should be raised from current levels, and if so, should Congress limit the use of the H-1B program by placing additional restrictions on employers and companies. Holders of H-1B visas are foreign professionals hired on a temporary basis by U.S. employers.
They can only be hired for "specialty occupations," which are defined under the law as jobs that require a professional who has the equivalent of a bachelor's degree in their field of specialty.
Examples are doctors, engineers, professors, accountants, researchers, medical personnel and computer professionals. The subject of H-1B visas was most recently addressed by Congress in the American Competitiveness and Workforce Improvement Act of 1998 (ACWIA). The ACWIA increased the number of H-1B visas to be granted beginning in 1999. According to the ACWIA, no more than 115,000 visas would be granted for each Fiscal Year 1999 and 2000, 107,500 in 2001, and 65,000 after that. ACWIA also added new requirements for employers who use a higher percentage of H-1B workers, such as requiring high-use companies to first recruit in the United States, and not lay off American workers before using the H-1B program. ACWIA also stiffened the punishments for companies that violate the law. The new punishments include fines of up to $35,000, a three-year bar from participating in visa programs, and repaying salaries of any underpaid foreign professionals.
ACWIA further requires employers to pay a fee of $500 per visa to fund education and training programs for U.S. workers. U.S. employers use the H-1B program to hire foreign professionals with highly needed skills.
Employers typically hire H-1B professionals for three reasons: [] Needed skills: To obtain essential skills or rare and unique knowledge; [] Global market expertise: For their special expertise in overseas needs, markets, trends or distribution, allowing U.S. businesses to compete in global markets; [] Temporary shortages: To fill temporary shortages of needed skills.
If American companies are prevented from hiring essential people to fill critical positions, an increasing number of jobs dependent upon these positions will go unfilled each year, resulting in American jobs being lost and American projects losing out to foreign competition.
As the U.S. economy becomes increasingly global, H-1B professionals become ever more essential to San Diego's continued economic growth. Larry Fitch, president and chief executive officer of San Diego Workforce Partnership, Inc., states, "The only way we will be able to permanently resolve the issue of importing workers for high technology positions is by growing our own work force.
This will require a major commitment to and investment in our youth.
Unfortunately, this is a long-term solution, and the crisis is now.
We must continue to use H-1B visas in the short term, and ensure that we are accessing the training dollars that are available with the visas to upgrade the skills of our current workers." ACWIA increased the number of temporary visas through fiscal year 2001, while making significant changes to the program to enhance domestic work-force protections.
However, the increases mandated in ACWIA were insufficient; the H-1B visa cap was reached well before the end of Fiscal Year 1999 and was reached in March in Fiscal Year 2000. Kim Giannone, Employment Specialist, Insight Electronics LLC says, "We firmly support an increase, and even the removal of the limit on H-1Bs. We are an international company, and we have problems filling our technical and engineering positions in all areas.
It is very difficult (if not impossible) for us to fill our openings with the available U.S. workers." Among other things, the legislation before Congress would accomplish the following: [] Increase the H-1B visa cap to 200,000 or more for each year for the period FY 2000-2003. [] Exempt from the H-1B visa cap non-immigrants employed by higher educational institutions and research institutions, and foreign graduates of U.S. masters and doctoral degree programs sponsored by U.S. employers within six months of their graduation. [] Permit extensions of H-1B status past the six-year limit for H-1B beneficiaries who have immigrant visa applications pending. Raising the H-1B visa limitation will have an immediate and significant impact on the San Diego economy.
San Diego is one of the fastest growing regions in the country in the high-tech, biotechnology and research industries.
San Diego also is home for hundreds of start-up companies and relatively small businesses.
These industries have the greatest demand for the H-1B program. San Diego employers use the H-1B program to obtain skilled foreign professionals at a time when unemployment is at an all-time low and competition for skilled technical workers is at an all-time high. Increasing the H-1B visa cap without imposing restrictions on employers would greatly enhance the growth of such companies as well as encourage increased investment into the San Diego region.

Y2K fixes may open U.S. to risk, CIA analyst says
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 15, 1999 Wednesday


Copyright 1999 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: NEWS; Pg. A-1
Length: 1048 words
Byline: Bruce V. Bigelow, STAFF WRITER
Body


A CIA analyst responsible for watching the nation's computer infrastructure is quietly warning network administrators that America's reliance on foreign programmers to fix the Y2K computer glitch poses a security risk. Computer security experts have long warned of a "digital Pearl Harbor" in which an enemy could exploit America's reliance on computer technology to steal secrets or spread chaos by sabotaging critical computer networks. Now, the warnings issued by Terrill D. Maynard of the National Infrastructure Protection Center appear to reflect increasing worries among top U.S. officials about America's vulnerabilities to cyber attacks. In an article intended for system administrators, Maynard specifically warned that foreign programmers hired to fix Y2K software problems may have covertly left an open door for a future network attack. President Clinton established the protection center at the FBI last year in part to warn U.S. industry of such cyber threats. Maynard, chief of the NIPC's Analysis and Warning Section, could not be reached for comment yesterday.
FBI officials said he was out of town. Other computer security experts said the center's warning may be valid, although they have no way of knowing whether foreign operatives are actually exploiting the Y2K glitch. "Certainly there's been a bunch of stuff within the Department of Defense in recent weeks, basically directives saying that we had to take extra steps to address Y2K concerns," said Ron Broersma, a network security expert at SPAWAR, the Navy's San Diego-based Space and Naval Warfare Systems Command. Broersma said directives issued by Arthur L. Money, the Pentagon's top civilian for command, control, communications and intelligence, have sought to address Y2K problems on two fronts. One concern addressed the possibility of programming flaws that might give unauthorized users a "back door" access to restricted computer networks, or allow them to launch malicious code, Broersma said. The other concern raised the possibility that foreign attacks on computer networks might be disguised as a Y2K glitch. Maynard said Y2K work has given foreign programmers "unprecedented access" to U.S. computer systems.
That's because the job of checking millions of lines of computer code for the "Year 2000" date glitch has overwhelmed what Maynard calls "trusted" programmers. He also identified Israel and India as countries that might be most likely to exploit their role in providing Y2K services to U.S. industry and government. Citing publicly available reports, Maynard noted that both Israel and India have tried to obtain industrial or military secrets from the United States. Both countries also have been developing the computer-based capabilities to wage "cyberwar," and both countries are also major providers of Y2K services. Or, in other words, both countries have the motive, capability and opportunity to insert software instructions that could be used to manipulate some computer networks. Dan Geer of SystemExperts in Sudbury, Mass., said Maynard and others issuing such warnings are "the professional paranoids in this space." Geer added he was skeptical that such a covert campaign, even if mounted by a foreign government, could remain secret for long. Deborah Stagg, who oversaw Y2K work for the San Diego Data Processing Center, said she experienced no problems with the India software company that has completed its work on the city of San Diego's systems. "We thoroughly tested every condition with my users and with my core staff," Stagg said.
"Everything matched up perfectly." Stagg added: "I think this guy is just an alarmist.
You know, there are so many of them out there on this Y2K thing." But a former U.S. intelligence official, who agreed to speak only if unnamed, described Maynard as a longtime defense intelligence analyst. "These are prudent steps," the former official said, adding that the NIPC has been working to both understand the threat of computer-based sabotage and to lay plans for how the government should best respond. Such concerns have been frequently discussed by computer security experts in recent years. Just this spring, Department of Defense officials acknowledged that highly coordinated electronic attacks were targeting unclassified-but-critical computer networks at military sites throughout the United States. The months-long attacks, which appeared to originate in Russia, also hit networks operated by private research and development institutes, according to press reports. Maynard raised the Y2K issue last week in Arlington, Va., during a conference of military officials, computer security consultants and defense contractors. Yesterday, Maynard's warnings were delivered to a far larger audience when an article he wrote was posted on a Web site hosted by the SANS Institute, an international research and education cooperative for more than 60,000 systems administrators. "The unprecedented `trusted' system access given to untested foreign computer software development companies and programmers in the Year 2000 remediation effort has offered a unique opportunity for potential adversaries to implant malicious code," Maynard wrote. In addition to Israel and India, Maynard said France, Germany, Russia and Taiwan represent a "second tier" of countries with cyberwar capabilities that also have sought U.S. economic secrets.
These five countries have not been major sources of Y2K work, however. Maynard's comments may raise a new issue in the debate over visas granted to foreign workers under the H-1B program, intended by Congress to bolster the technical work force of America's high-tech companies. "It's not a realistic look at the population or the people who come here" under the H-1B program, said Grace Zimmerman of Larrabee and Associates, a San Diego law firm specializing exclusively in immigration law for corporate clients. Generally, these are foreign workers who come to the United States to attend college and they want to stay, Zimmerman said. "It's certainly not within their best interests to engage in this kind of espionage or destruction of systems, for which they can be criminally prosecuted," the lawyer said.
"These are people who are coming here to establish a life for themselves in the United States."

INS handed out high-tech visas lavishly; May have exceeded legal cap by as many as 20,000
The San Diego Union-Tribune
October 9, 1999 Saturday


Copyright 1999 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: NEWS; Pg. A-1
Length: 724 words
Byline: Diane Lindquist, COPLEY NEWS SERVICE
Dateline: WASHINGTON 
Body


WASHINGTON -- The Immigration and Naturalization Service has exceeded this year's limit on visas for temporary high-tech foreign workers, granting as many as 10,000 to 20,000 above the 115,000 limit set by Congress. An INS spokeswoman yesterday confirmed a miscount in the number of visas approved.
The agency has hired an outside auditor to determine how far over the legal cap it went because the agency itself remains uncertain. "We do know we went over.
But, at this point, we're not speculating by how many.
That's part of what the audit is supposed to find out," said the spokeswoman, Eyleen Schmidt. Schmidt said the agency first noticed "anomalies" in the numbers in June. INS officials disclosed the problem to House and Senate staffers last week. They told the staffers the range of extra visas was 10,000 to 20,000, making the total as much as 10 percent above the legal cap. Awarding too many of the controversial H-1B visas, dubbed high-tech visas because of that industry's vigorous effort last year to nearly double the number granted, is likely to bolster efforts to reform the agency, which controls foreigners' legal and illegal entry into the country. The INS is trying to stave off legislation that would break it into two separate agencies within the Justice Department.
Other proposals call for dismantling it and spreading its parts even farther, including some to the State Department and others to Labor. "The (INS') problems are getting bigger, not smaller," said Allen Kay, a spokesman for Rep. Lamar Smith, R-Texas, chairman of the House immigration subcommittee, yesterday.
"The INS is required to obey the law like everyone else." The subcommittee will almost certainly hold oversight hearings to examine the matter, he added. Among questions that need to be answered, he said, are how too many visas were granted, and why, when the INS first noticed a problem in June, it failed to report the miscalculation until last week. Smith was dismayed and upset when subcommittee staffers told him of the miscalculation, Kay said. "The first word out of his mouth was `incompetence,' " Kay said. Suggestions on correcting the problem are coming from Capitol Hill as well as the INS, but no agreement has been reached. "At this point in time, rescinding the visas is not one of the options we're exploring," INS spokeswoman Schmidt said. Options that are being considered include laying the excess visas off on another fiscal year, including fiscal year 2000. Another suggestion has been to assign them to earlier fiscal years in which the cap was not reached, she said. Four service processing centers -- located in Vermont, Texas, Nebraska and in California at Laguna Niguel -- count the H-1B applications as they are approved.
The information is forwarded periodically to a mainframe computer at INS headquarters in Washington. Somehow, Schmidt said, not all approvals were counted by the mainframe. Congress created the H-1B visa program in 1990 to allow foreigners with a bachelor's degree or higher, or distinguished merit or ability, to work in the United States.
In the first six years of the program, the numbers never reached the annual cap of 65,000. But in 1997 the cap was reached for the first time.
And the high-tech industry stepped up its efforts to substantially raise or even remove the cap.
In 1998, Congress temporarily raised the number of visas to 115,000 for fiscal years 1999 and 2000, and to 107,500 for fiscal year 2001. After that, it is to return to 65,000. Critics of the H-1B program strenuously fought the high-tech industry's efforts, arguing that many U.S. programmers were jobless.
They said the industry was turning to foreign programmers and engineers as a way to get cheaper workers. With the raising of the cap, employers were required to pay a $500 fee that helps fund training and educational programs for U.S. workers. This past June, the INS stopped accepting applications for 1999 H-1Bs, saying they had received enough to fill the cap.
In August, the agency announced it had reached the limit. Now, it appears, it may have reached it earlier. Of the 108,386 visas granted by June, the agency said, 46 percent were from India, 10 percent from China, 4 percent from Canada and 3 percent from the Philippines.
Other countries included Taiwan, Korea, Japan, the United Kingdom, Pakistan and Russia.

Letters to the Editor
The San Diego Union-Tribune
October 14, 1999 Thursday


Copyright 1999 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. B-15
Length: 1320 words
Body


This program creates indentured servants Qualcomm continually complains about a worker shortage, even though it recently laid off several hundred people.
It's interesting to note that late last year, Qualcomm was begging Uncle Sam to allow more foreign workers into the country through the H-1B program. Despite frequently submitting my resume to Qualcomm while constantly improving my skills and revising my resume, it has never even bothered to give me a phone interview.
And it keeps complaining about a shortage of workers?
I finally reached the conclusion that Qualcomm probably doesn't treat its employees any better than it treats their candidates. In the case of other local employers, most of the people doing the interviewing don't realize that most competent programmers can easily learn new languages once they have mastered the art of coding. In my current job, I'm now developing dynamic web content using PERL and JavaScript.
It's interesting to note that I had absolutely no prior work experience nor training in these particular programming languages.
Many employers will turn down candidates unless they have precisely the right experience and key words on their resume, however. Is there really a shortage, or is corporate America not willing to be more open-minded in its recruiting efforts? The high-tech industry continues to proclaim that recent college graduates are earning salaries averaging $47,000. I earned my B.S. in computer science in 1995, have been constantly updating my skills, and always remain dedicated to my work.
As of today, I make not a penny over $35,000 per year as a mid-level webmaster with a large, international company.
Perhaps there is actually a glut of talent in San Diego? Perhaps the so-called shortage is just a ruse to get Uncle Sam to approve even more guest worker visas.
As for immigration, I have no problem with granting permanent citizenship to foreigners who can contribute to this country.
On the other hand, the H-1B program merely creates indentured servants. KEVIN C. KEY San Diego Downtown ballpark process roils the public After attending my first City Council meeting Oct. 5, I came away wondering. As Congress reconvenes, lots of high-tech issues to tackle
The San Diego Union-Tribune
January 25, 2000, Tuesday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: COMPUTER LINK;Pg. 10
Length: 814 words
Byline: Marilyn Geewax; COX NEWS SERVICE
Body

Last year, Congress got beyond its partisan rancor long enough to pass a number of laws sought by the nation's booming high-tech companies.  This year, the tech titans expect even more out of Washington.
On trade, taxes, visas, digital signatures and many other issues, the owners of technology-driven companies will be pushing for far-reaching bills, with the 106th Congress reconvening this week.
With lawmakers planning to recess in late July to attend political party conventions and launch re-election campaigns, the pressure to get bills passed early in the year will be intense.
"You can't just say, 'OK, this is a short year, so let's just put these bills in a drawer,' " said Rep. Bob Goodlatte, R-Va., co-chair of the Congressional Internet Caucus.
Rather than dismiss 2000 as a do-nothing election year, tech supporters will "be pushing every step of the way," he said.  "You have to build momentum."
Now it's hot
Until 1998, technology-related issues were not particularly hot topics in Washington, both because most lawmakers were unfamiliar with the Internet and because many tech entrepreneurs were too busy making money to care about politics.
But as the Web has woven itself into the workplaces and homes of tens of millions of Americans, tech legislative issues have shot to the top of the agenda on the Hill.
And in places like Austin, Palo Alto, Boston, Seattle, Atlanta and Northern Virginia, entrepreneurs now realize their fortunes may be determined in large part by Congress's action or lack of it.
With tech companies hiring lobbyists at a frantic pace and lawmakers eagerly listening to them, bills have been moving along in a surprisingly bipartisan fashion.
In less than two years, the Republican-led Congress and Democratic White House have agreed to impose a three-year moratorium on taxes on electronic commerce, increase the number of visas issued to highly skilled immigrants, limit lawsuits against companies involved with Y2K-related software flaws, and extend copyright protections to material published on the Web.
This year, the tech agenda is even more aggressive.
Of course, electronic-commerce companies, chip makers and other sectors of the diverse high-tech business community have different priorities, but companies propelled by new technologies generally want lawmakers to help them by:
[] Expanding global trade
Since 1992, high-tech products have been the leading U.S. exports.
To increase growth, computer makers, chip manufacturers, software developers and others want greater access to foreign markets, especially China.
Tech companies want Congress to grant permanent normal trade relations status to China.
That's not a simple matter in an election year because labor unions, a core Democrat constituency, oppose expanded trade.  So do some conservatives who believe China never lives up to its agreements.
Tech companies also are demanding that government further loosen controls on the export of high-performance computers.
Again, this is a touchy issue because many legislators fear that equipment vital to our national security will end up illegally in the hands of Chinese military officials.
[] Increasing the pool of highly skilled workers
Employers are desperate for more engineers and programmers.  They want Congress to increase the number of "H-1B" temporary work visas used by companies and universities to hire foreign workers with specialized skills.
Industry was successful in boosting the visa level two years ago, but opposition from labor unions will be stiff in this election year.  Anti-immigration Republicans may oppose the visa increase as well.
Tech employers also want the government to bolster U.S. schools by providing development grants for teachers who want more computer training and scholarships for students pursuing technology-related degrees.
[] Spurring research and development
Tech-driven entrepreneurs want a permanent extension of the research and development tax credit.
[] Encouraging the growth of electronic commerce
The biggest issue is whether to allow state and local governments to collect sales taxes from online purchases.
Currently, most shoppers pay no sales taxes on Internet purchases because of a temporary tax moratorium imposed by Congress.  But as e-commerce grows, more mayors and governors are clamoring for the right to collect sales taxes.
This may be too hot of a potato for Congress to hold in an election year, so the moratorium may be extended to bump the issue into 2001.
But Sen. John McCain, R-Ariz., a vocal opponent of Internet taxes, could push for a permanent ban to attract attention to his presidential campaign.
Tech companies also want Congress to move on a number of other issues, such as approving the use of electronic signatures to allow more business dealings over the Internet, and settling a number of thorny privacy issues involving transfers of financial data and medical records.

High-tech workers are a visa away; In time of need, firms look to foreign shores
The San Diego Union-Tribune
February 13, 2000, Sunday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. I-1; STATISTICS.
Length: 967 words
Byline: Bradley Brooks; ASSOCIATED PRESS
Body

NEW YORK -- Oliver Sharp constantly worries about where he'll find his next hire.
As the chief technology officer of iTurf.com, which is set to launch a Web portal aimed at teens and their spending power, Sharp is obsessed with finding qualified high-tech workers in the tightest of tight labor markets.
"When I came to iTurf, nothing mattered except assembling a team," Sharp says.  "It was an absolute occupation of mine and it remains one today."
The issue faced by Sharp and others like him has raised enough concerns to get the ear of Congress.
Nineteen senators have introduced a bill to increase the number of H-1B visas -- six-year, temporary visas granted to foreigners with college degrees -- to 195,000 for the next three years.
Congress already has increased the number of H-1B visas from 65,000 in fiscal 1998 to 115,000 annually for both 1999 and 2000.
Many in the industry, however, say the H-1B visa doesn't go far enough to help the technology industry, as the H-1B visa is available for all professionals, from lawyers to physical therapists to fashion models.
About half of all H-1B visa requests for 1999 came from outside the technology industry, according to the Labor Department.
For that reason, Sen. Charles Robb, D-Va., introduced the HITEC Act in late September that would create a visa -- the T-visa -- just for foreign technology workers.  It is now before the Judiciary Committee.
"The U.S. pushed globalization, but somewhere along the line the trade advocates forgot to tell immigration," says Paul O'Malley, a New York attorney specializing in immigration.  "This industry changes much faster than the legal parameters for visas or green cards."
For example, in fiscal year 1999 ending Sept. 30, the quota for the H-1B visa was reached by mid-June, and O'Malley fears it will be reached much sooner this year.
Those opposed to allowing more foreign workers into the United States say high-tech companies simply want to take advantage of less-costly foreign workers, perhaps only hiring them temporarily.
Immigrants come to the United States, says Paul Donnelly, with the Immigration Reform Coalition, because they want to be permanent residents.  Yet high-tech companies have no intentions of sponsoring them for green cards that would give them more freedom to move to a different job.
O'Malley says the temporary hiring charge is right, that technology companies often work on a project-by-project basis, and resistance to that is an example of an antiquated notion of how the industry works.
The T-visa legislation addresses the allegation that technology companies are seeking foreign workers at lower wages by requiring that a job offer a salary of at least $60,000.
Workers would be required to hold a master's or doctoral degree in mathematics, science, engineering or computer science from a U.S. university.
Businesses would pay $1,000 for each T-visa, which would be good for five years, and $500 for a five-year extension.
Despite disagreement over the solution, most parties at least agree that there is a problem finding technology workers.
A report released by the Office of Technology Policy within the Department of Commerce indicates symptoms of a tight technology labor market exist: low unemployment, high salaries and an expanding market.
The Computing Technology Industry Association, an industry trade group, claims that the United States needs 268,000 high-tech workers to solve a problem the association says costs U.S. businesses $4.5 billion a year in lost productivity.
Even the addition of the T-visa or an increase in H-1B visas might not allow enough skilled workers in.
The Commerce report notes that about 32 percent of core technology workers don't have a four-year college degree.  In addition, many high-tech workers who have a degree got it in a subject other than that required by the T-visa legislation.
Take the case of Ehud Lavski of Tel Aviv, Israel, who would like to work in the United States.
At 25, he has seven years experience in new-media work, giving him veteran status in the industry.  He's designed several CD-ROMs. He did a stint with a multimedia company.  He's been a free-lance graphic designer, directed music videos and has written and illustrated a children's book.
In short, he's a talent that most new media companies would salivate over.  His one drawback: He doesn't hold a degree.
"I had this thought that if you're good enough and a company wants you, that it would be instantaneous.  I was a little naive," Lavski says.  "People can't hire me based on my talent."
For now, Lavski said he and his brother are going into business together producing CD-ROMs in Israel.
One option that the Commerce report found hard-pressed companies taking is the outsourcing of their lower-level programming to other countries, where it can be done cheaply.
Hussam Hamadeh, a co-founder of Vault.com, a job-search Web site, says his company would prefer to keep programming on-site, even though it would cost $100 an hour vs. $20 an hour in India.
However, he says, Vault.com has 12 core technology workers and needs to fill seven more positions.  "There are a lot of new features we want to add to the site that we just can't add," Hamadeh says.
As a result, Vault.com reluctantly is considering outsourcing as an option.
"It is easier and more efficient than having someone overseas who will send the work back, and then you realize that they might not have understood the project," Hamadeh says, echoing a common concern in the industry.
For Sharp at iTurf, the issue of whether more foreign technology workers should be in the United States is a no-brainer, complexities of the situation aside.
"To the extent that talented, ambitious people want to come to work in the U.S.," Sharp says.  "I say, hallelujah.  Let the skies rain those people down on us."

1 GRAPH; SOURCE: American Electronics Association, Congressional staff | ASSOCIATED PRESS; High-tech demand (I-7)

Company: AMERICAN ELECTRONICS ASSOCIATION   (59%);  AMERICAN ELECTRONICS ASSOCIATION  (59%)

Industry: PASSPORTS & VISAS (99%); EMPLOYMENT VISAS (90%); WEBSITES & PORTALS (77%); YOUTH MARKET (77%); LAWYERS (73%); HEALTH CARE PROFESSIONALS (66%)

BUSINESS BRIEFING
The San Diego Union-Tribune
March 16, 2000, Thursday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. C-2
Length: 725 words
Byline: Compiled from staff and wire reports
Body

SAN DIEGO AND CALIFORNIA
Vantas acquires Western Business
New York-based Vantas Inc. has acquired Carlsbad-based Western Business Centers for an undisclosed price, giving it control of six office buildings, including two in San Diego and one in Carlsbad.  Western Business, a suite management company, leases space and provides business services.  In San Diego, Vantas takes over the Rio Vista Tower in Mission Valley and Emerald Plaza downtown.  In Carlsbad, it acquired the Carlsbad Pacific Center on Palomar Airport Road. Vantas now owns 28 office building in the Los Angeles and San Diego metropolitan areas.
Gateway exec sells part of stake
Ted Waitt, chairman of San Diego-based Gateway, sold 1.1 million shares of the company's common stock in February, according to a form released yesterday by the Securities and Exchange Commission.  Waitt sold his shares between Feb. 1 and Feb. 23 at prices ranging from $53.44 a share to $63.25 a share.  He still owns more than 114.1 million Gateway shares.  Gateway is a direct-seller of personal computers.
Titan to buy Maryland company
San Diego-based Titan Corp. said it has agreed to buy closely held Pulse Engineering, a small defense contractor in Hanover, Md., that specializes in intelligence and cryptographic technologies.  The Maryland business is unrelated to the former Pulse Engineering of San Diego, which was acquired by Technitrol in 1995. Pulse's 180 employees will become a part of Titan Systems Corp., and will continue to work in Maryland.  Financial terms were not disclosed.  Titan is a diversified information-technology products and services company.
China premier denies CDMA delays
Chinese Premier Zhu Rongji said yesterday that "it's not true" China has suspended or delayed deployment of code division multiple access or CDMA, the wireless technology Qualcomm developed.  Qualcomm CEO Irwin Jacobs recently told shareholders that he was puzzled by reports last month that China had pulled the plug on CDMA, an apparent political victim of flagging U.S. enthusiasm for China's entry into the World Trade Organization.  Qualcomm is counting on China to boost global growth of CDMA, which it licenses.  But Zhu told reporters yesterday that "What they (reports) are saying is not true and there has never been anything like that."
Sempra unit wins stadium pact
Sempra Energy Services has won a $25 million contract to design and build a heating and cooling plant for the new NFL/Rodeo Stadium in Houston.  Sempra Energy Services is a unit of Sempra Energy, parent company of SDG&E and Southern California Gas Co.
WORLD AND NATION
Bill would raise ceiling on visas
As expected, lawmakers from areas growing from the influx of new high-tech companies introduced legislation yesterday to nearly double the number of visas issued each year for tech-savvy foreign workers.  Their bill would raise from 115,000 to 200,000 the ceiling on the six-year, H-1B visas the Immigration and Naturalization Services now issues annually to professional workers from overseas.  Claiming there are now 364,000 more high-tech jobs than workers to fill them, Rep. David Drier, R-Calif., said the present ceiling doesn't meet the needs of an increasingly digitalized economy.
Sears CEO plans to retire
Arthur Martinez plans to retire as Sears, Roebuck and Co. chairman and chief executive by year-end, and the giant retailer has hired an executive recruiting firm to help find a successor, Sears said yesterday.  Martinez, 60, discussed his retirement plans with the Sears board of directors earlier this year and started the process of choosing a successor, the company said.
Decisions due in generic drug cases
U.S. antitrust officials are expected to announce a settlement with Novartis AG's Geneva Pharmaceuticals Inc. unit and Abbott Laboratories over claims the companies agreed to keep a generic drug off the market, people familiar with the case said yesterday.  At the same time, the U.S. Federal Trade Commission is expected to announce a vote approving an administrative complaint against Andrx Corp. and Aventis SA stemming from similar allegations, the people said.  Both cases are part of the commission's investigation into charges that brand-name drugmakers conspired to keep cheaper generic drugs off the market.  The FTC will not collect or seek financial penalties in either case, the sources said.

March 16, 2000 Thursday

At the same time, the U.S. Federal Trade Commission is expected to announce a vote approving an administrative complaint against Andrx Corp. and Aventis SA stemming from similar allegations, the people said.
Both cases are part of the commission's investigation into charges that brand-name drugmakers conspired to keep cheaper generic drugs off the market.
The FTC will not collect or seek financial penalties in either case, the sources said.
