Bill offers unlimited visas for high tech
The San Diego Union-Tribune
April 12, 2000, Wednesday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. C-2
Length: 409 words
Byline: Michelle Mittelstadt; ASSOCIATED PRESS
Body

WASHINGTON -- The chairman of the House immigration subcommittee yesterday introduced legislation that would allow an unlimited number of visas over the next three years for foreigners with prized high-tech skills.
The measure offered by Rep. Lamar Smith is far broader than a version he offered just weeks ago that would have provided a one-time increase of 45,000 visas in the program.
The Texas Republican said his plan would address the booming high-tech industry's plaint of persistent labor shortages.  At a news conference, Smith said he expanded his bill after learning that this year's demand for visas outpaced last year's request by 50,000.
Industry officials, who support other House and Senate proposals that would raise the H-1B visa program beyond its current 115,000 annual limit, said Smith's bill is laced with anti-business provisions -- and they questioned whether, if implemented, it would result in any increases.
"Maybe he just felt the tide swinging dramatically against him, and he thought if he put out a large number that people wouldn't notice there were so many strings attached to it," said Harris Miller, president of the Information Technology Association of America.
The provisions in Smith's bill disliked by high-tech interests include language requiring companies petitioning for H-1B visas to show they've increased their U.S. work force and raised salaries for American workers.  The bill also requires companies to file public reports on the foreign workers and their pay.
Smith's spokesman, Allen Kay, rejected the criticisms, calling the proposal "a fantastic pro-business bill."
The industry backs a bill drafted by the chairmen of the Senate Judiciary Committee and its immigration subcommittee that would boost the H-1B program to 195,000 visas annually over three years.  Also favored is a House version offered by Reps. David Dreier, R-Calif., and Zoe Lofgren, D-Calif., that would raise the ceiling to 200,000 annually over three years.
Dreier said he welcomes Smith's newest legislation, which will be voted on today by the House immigration subcommittee.
The high-tech sector claims it requires hundreds of thousands of new workers immediately -- a contention disputed by organized labor, which argues the industry is looking overseas chiefly to hold down wages.
Lawmakers have heeded the industry's complaints about work force shortages before.
Two years ago, Congress agreed to temporarily increase the H-1B program.

Rise sought in skilled foreigners
The San Diego Union-Tribune
May 12, 2000, Friday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: NEWS;Pg. A-7
Length: 134 words
Byline: The New York Times News Service
Body

WASHINGTON -- Racing to keep up with a booming economy, President Clinton yesterday proposed an increase of more than 85 percent in the number of skilled foreign workers who could be admitted to the United States to fill job openings in high-technology industries.
Clinton asked Congress to provide 200,000 visas for such workers in each of the next three years.
The existing law, adopted in 1998, limits the number of such visas to 107,500 in 2001. After that, under existing law, the ceiling will fall back to 65,000 a year.
The president reluctantly supported the much more modest increase in so-called H-1B visas made in 1998. But since then, technology companies say, the demand for computer programmers, engineers and other skilled workers has outstripped the supply and exceeded official expectations.

Immigration and America's promise
The San Diego Union-Tribune
May 19, 2000 Friday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. B-8:2,7; B-14:1
Length: 859 words
Byline: Jack Kemp, COPLEY NEWS SERVICE, Kemp is co-director of Empower America and Distinguished Fellow of the Competitive Enterprise Institute.
Body


Now, as always, immigrants have been a vital part of our nation's economic, political and social development.
That's why our friends and neighbors abroad may wonder why immigration policy in the United States, the ultimate nation of immigrants, has not only been contentious, but oftentimes acrimonious. Like every important issue in a free, democratic nation, the immigration debate reflects a healthy tension that resolves itself through the political process.
Citizens who may see their economic interests jeopardized by new immigrants have the power to shape the laws that protect those interests, while the nation as a whole continues to enjoy the long-term benefits of our openness to new immigrants.
That means U.S. immigration law may not be a model of logical consistency and legal elegance, but it works. It also means, however, that few people are ever satisfied with our immigration laws, which remain in a constant state of evolution.
As is the case with our tax code, immigration rules and procedures are shaped ad hoc, responding to the crisis of the moment. A case in point is the H-1B visa program, an exception to the general framework of U.S. immigration policy that allows us to admit more highly skilled immigrants to meet the economic needs of high-tech industries experiencing critical shortages of workers with specialized training.
Right now, there is wide support in Congress to increase the number of workers allowed temporary entry to the United States under H-1B status to fill jobs which are vital to our continued economic expansion. This is an important issue because high-tech is one area where we need as free-flowing a labor market as we can get.
But it's not the only issue on the immigration agenda.
There is also a need for some legislative "clean-up" provisions to rescue from bureaucratic limbo workers already here, on the job and helping to make our economy grow.
These clean-up reforms are just good sense and don't require any change in basic immigration law or an increase in the overall number of legal immigrants. The heart-wrenching case of Elian Gonzalez, the 6-year-old Cuban boy rescued at sea after his mother and nine others drowned attempting to escape from Cuba on a raft, riveted the nation's attention on just one example of the lengths to which people will go to escape tyranny and poverty and seek a better life in America. Today in America there are thousands of other cases, if not as well-known as Elian's, which carry into the 21st century the moving narrative of immigrants abandoning their homes and struggling to make a new life in this land we like to think of as a Shining City on a Hill. Four simple adjustments to the law I have in mind deal exclusively with working aliens already in the United States -- workers from war-torn Central American countries -- who have established a solid track record as law-abiding citizens supporting families and paying taxes, and close family members who seek to rejoin immigrant workers who are either U.S. citizens or permanent residents.
All of these reforms would promote traditional American values such as diligence, hard work, family unity and putting down roots. These reforms would allow Salvadorans, Guatemalans, Hondurans and Haitians to apply for document status just as already has been done for Cubans and Nicaraguans; allow undocumented workers of good character who have resided in the U.S. many years to apply for documented status; allow those who have lost their status as legal residents, but remain eligible to become permanent residents, to remain in the U.S. while seeking to regain their status; and help reduce family backlogs by providing more visas for close family members of citizens and permanent residents. Immigration laws establish the legal framework that allows Congress and the courts to control the flow of immigrants and legal aliens into the United States.
But the indomitable human spirit doesn't first hire a lawyer before fleeing a situation it finds intolerable and getting on a raft or swimming a river to freedom and opportunity in America. As Ronald Reagan reminded us in speaking of the Statue of Liberty's torch, "The glistening hope of that lamp is still ours.
Every promise, every opportunity is still golden in this land.
And through that golden door our children can walk into tomorrow with the knowledge that no one can be denied the promise that is America." The fact is, in a land as free and open as ours, there always will be more people who want to enter than we can accommodate under our laws.
That is a profound vote of confidence in the American dream. And just as surely, there always will be a certain percentage who succeed in forging a life in the U.S. and assimilate into our society sufficiently to become, after many years here, "citizens" in all but name -- what one might think of as "common-law" citizens -- as much citizens of this land as were many of our immigrant forefathers.
These true stakeholders in America deserve a chance to gain full citizenship status, and that obliges us to review and adjust our laws on occasion to make that possible.
To do anything less is to dim Liberty's lamp.
Classification

Quest for more foreign high-tech workers runs into hurdle
The San Diego Union-Tribune
May 26, 2000 Friday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS; Pg. C-1
Length: 285 words
Byline: Joe Cantlupe, COPLEY NEWS SERVICE
Dateline: WASHINGTON 
Body


WASHINGTON -- Clinton administration officials complicated a high-tech industry push for access to more foreign workers yesterday when they said an 18-month-old law to protect U.S. technology workers has not been implemented. "This is a horror," said Rep. Sheila Jackson Lee, D-Texas. The disclosures came during a hearing before the House immigration subcommittee on efforts to expand the H-1B visa program.
High-tech employers have turned to the program with greater frequency in recent years to recruit foreign tech workers for their U.S. plants. In October 1998, Congress enacted a law tightening requirements on businesses to recruit U.S. workers before hiring foreign workers and barring employers from laying off U.S. workers and replacing them with foreign workers. But, 18 months later, it remains unclear when the new regulatory protections will be in place, said officials with the White House Office of Management and Budget and the Labor Department. "I am appalled," said subcommittee Chairman Lamar Smith, R-Texas. Nonetheless, some expansion of the foreign visa program is expected this year. OMB administrator John T. Spotila defended the delays. "Achieving a proper balance between the needs of U.S. workers and those of U.S. businesses has been an important focus of administration policy," Spotila said. San Diego giant Qualcomm and other high-tech employers have pressed Congress to expand the H-1B program to offset what they say is a shortage of U.S. high-tech workers. Earlier this month, the House Judiciary Committee approved a bill sponsored by Smith that would allow an unlimited number of H-1B visas between 2001 and 2002. The current annual cap of 115,000 was reached several months ago.

Congress OKs more visas for high-tech talent
The San Diego Union-Tribune
October 4, 2000, Wednesday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: NEWS;Pg. A-1
Length: 898 words
Byline: Bart Jansen; ASSOCIATED PRESS
Body

WASHINGTON -- High-tech companies could bring in almost 600,000 additional skilled foreign workers over the next three years under a bill that sped through Congress yesterday.
The Senate passed the bill on a 96-1 vote, and the House followed with voice vote passage several hours later, abandoning a version of its own that met serious opposition from the technology industry.
The quick congressional action after nine months of jockeying fulfills an election-year promise by both Democratic and Republican leaders to a high-tech sector that is increasingly flexing its political muscle through lobbying and campaign donations to both parties.  President Clinton has said he will sign it.
"We should pass the bill just as it is, so we don't have to conference it," said Rep. Zoe Lofgren, D-Calif.  "It's the best bill that's been considered yet."
Lofgren had co-sponsored a similar bill with Rep. David Dreier, R-Calif.  Their bill had stalled in the House in favor of a version that had more protections for American high-tech workers.
That version, by Rep. Lamar Smith, R-Texas, was approved by the House Judiciary Committee and would have required employers to pay the immigrants at least $40,000 a year and not use them to replace Americans on their payrolls.  Companies also would have to document that they had at least $250,000 in capital to participate in the program.
The industry opposed Smith's bill.
With U.S. unemployment rates holding steady near a 30-year low, companies in California's Silicon Valley and along other booming high-tech corridors say they need the additional workers with six-year H-1B visas to fuel their continuing rapid growth.
"The short-term problem is how to fill the key positions immediately so that we don't lose opportunities to foreign competitors or so that we don't force American businesses to move offshore to where skilled workers might live," said Sen. Spencer Abraham, R-Mich.
"In the short term this is good news for us," said Tony Chartrand, vice president of human resources for Ericsson's San Diego wireless unit.  "Longer term, it's not the ultimate solution."
Ericsson currently has about 200 of its 1,300 local employees under visas, which includes about 175 the company inherited when it took over Qualcomm's infrastructure unit in May 1999.
Chartrand said he doesn't expect that Ericsson will change its recruiting strategy, even with the boost in the quota.  Of the 200 workers the company typically hires each year, about 10 percent work under temporary visas, which have a maximum length of six years.
Qualcomm, another aggressive supporter of the expanded quota, welcomed the action.
"The company's success is built on the ability to attract and retain leading engineers from inside the United States and elsewhere," company spokeswoman Christine Trimble said.  "This legislation allows Qualcomm and other technology companies increased access to worldwide engineering talent."
Other industry advocates -- including Microsoft and Sun Microsystems, the U.S. Chamber of Commerce and the National Association of Manufacturers -- also praised yesterday's action.
"If we want the United States to maintain its technological advantage, employers must be allowed to hire the professionals they need," said Paula Collins, director of government relations for Texas Instruments.
Technology businesses have more than doubled their political contributions during the past two years, according to the independent Center for Responsive Politics.  The companies have given candidates $22.1 million since the start of last year, with Democrats getting a little more than half of that.
Computer software and other high-tech companies contend that 300,000 jobs are going unfilled for a lack of qualified workers.  Labor unions, however, argue that the companies want more immigrants so as to keep down wages of Americans holding the same jobs.
Sen. Ernest F. Hollings, D-S.C., cast the lone vote against the bill.  Sens. Dianne Feinstein, D-Calif.; Edward M. Kennedy, D-Mass., and Joseph Lieberman, D-Conn., did not vote.
The bill allows the Immigration and Naturalization Service to issue up to 195,000 new H-1B visas annually for the next three years to skilled foreign workers.  The bill also would exempt from the cap foreign graduates of U.S. master's or doctoral programs or foreign workers at U.S. colleges, providing another source of labor to high-tech companies.
Exempted from the caps are foreign employees of universities and higher education research facilities.  But dropped from the legislation was language in an earlier version that would also have exempted from the ceilings foreign students with graduate degrees from U.S. universities who are subsequently hired by American high-tech companies.
Under present law, the government issued 115,000 H-1B visas during the fiscal year that ended Saturday.  With no new legislation the ceiling would fall to 107,500 this year and to 65,000 next year.
Democrats in both chambers said last week they will try to put other immigration measures, which had slowed consideration of the visa bill, on one of the spending bills that Congress must pass before adjourning for the year, under threat of a presidential veto.
The measures include granting amnesty to illegal immigrants who arrived in the United States before 1987 and offering permanent residency to more political refugees from Central America and Haiti.

LETTERS
The San Diego Union-Tribune
October 22, 2000, Sunday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION;Pg. G-3; LETTER TO ED.
Length: 1309 words
Body

John Moores deserves support of community
I cannot believe the hogwash being spoken about John Moores, owner of the San Diego Padres.  Since his arrival from Houston, this man has supported our city on so much more than just a baseball level.  I have heard and read of critics blasting him for contributing a huge amount of money into our school system.
How jaded are we as a city to believe that this man has only contributed this money to somehow further a political agenda?  Moores has nothing to gain by giving money to our schools.  Our children, on the other hand, reap the massive benefits of his generosity in the way of a better education.  As a parent, I thank him.
I also have heard and read the critics of the downtown ballpark, and I ask one simple question.  Who in their right mind would want that area to remain as it is?  The area is a mass of run-down old buildings that smell of human excrement.
By contrast, picture the revenue of a ballpark, the jobs it would open up for those who need them, as well as simple pride we would feel as a community by housing a major league club in a major league ballpark.  I cannot understand how we could not want this to happen.  Moores and Larry Lucchino have tried to better our city and have been personally and financially blasted for it.  Why?
Is it the fact that we already have Qualcomm Stadium and it is believed to be enough?  If we want the growth potential and financial gains of having a major league baseball club, we had better be prepared to act like a major league city.
The people of this wonderful city need to take an active and aggressive approach on this issue.  Moores needs to know that there are people in San Diego standing with him in his quest to better our city and are outraged that Bruce Henderson, specifically, continues to waste our time and money in throwing up these roadblocks.  I would imagine that if the opposition had to fund its own litigation, this would have been a very short-lived fight. ANDREA HOWELL Escondido
Credit for Milosevic's fall should go to Clinton
Re: "He's Gone, The end of the Milosevic era": (G-5, Oct. 15)
It is interesting that Ivo Daalder does not mention that President Clinton is principally responsible for the downfall of Slobodan Milosevic.  The president led our NATO allies in a bombing campaign that ended Milosevic's Hitler-like brutal ethnic cleansing.  This was accomplished without the loss of a single American life.
This was accomplished in spite of dire warnings that Milosevic's anti-aircraft capability was predicted to be far superior to Saddam Hussein's and that our pilots would be in jeopardy.  This was accomplished in spite of warnings by our military that we would not be able to stop Milosevic's genocide without a costly ground war.
This was accomplished in spite of the predictions of doom and gloom by the talking heads on radio talk shows, the TV and the press.
Is it unreasonable to give credit to the Clinton administration just because we have an election looming? NORMAN E. TIPTON El Cajon
Immigrant visa bill will cause problems
The passage of the bill by Congress to increase the H-1B visas authorizing 190,000 new foreign high-tech workers per year to enter the workplace demonstrates that, when it's a choice between the average American's interests and those of deep-pocket industries, we lose.
The high-tech industry's vigorous opposition to the shelved Senate version, requiring an annual salary of $40,000 and stipulating that foreign workers could not be used to replace American workers, proves the intent is to import cheap foreign labor.  When jobs become scarce, and they will at some point, are the low-paid foreign workers going to be let go?  This is an ill-conceived, short-term fix, which will negatively impact future Americans.
In the next three years, almost 600,000 new workers will enter this country through this program.  Add to this family members, spouses and children, and it can easily reach 1.2 million.  That's about the population of San Diego.  The six-year limit is a joke.  Most of these will apply for permanent residence.
Probably the most immediate impact we can all relate to is sitting in our car, on our gridlocked freeways, smelling the aroma of countless exhausts, while we gaze at the sight of every available hill being graded flat for new housing.  San Diego, being one of the high-tech centers, can be assured of filling those houses.  One can only imagine what it will be like for our children and grandchildren. LILIANE LOUVET Escondido
SDSU alumni want Aztec mascot kept
One of the greatest traditions of San Diego is under attack.  If the citizens of San Diego County don't stand up and stop this attack, we will lose what few traditions we have.  I am talking about the Aztec tradition of San Diego State University.
Few things in society have ever received as much support as the retention of Monty Montezuma and the Aztec tradition.  If a poll were conducted and asked what color the sky is, it is doubtful that there would be as much consensus as there is on the Aztec mascot.
The SDSU Alumni Association sent out 12,000 e-mails asking whether the Aztec name should be kept.  Of the 4,000 responses received, 98 percent supported keeping the Aztec tradition as part of San Diego.  Due to that overwhelming support by the alumni, the Alumni Association resolved that the Aztec name, logo and human mascot should be kept.
With all this support, why is there still the possibility of Monty Montezuma becoming extinct?  Because a few students at San Diego State have called the Aztec tradition "racist" and "culturally degrading." That small group of students could not be further from the truth.  Ash Hayes, former president of the Alumni Association, said, "the Aztec tradition is based on honor, vigor, glory and heritage." That is truly what the Aztec tradition of SDSU is all about.
We must fight to keep that tradition of honor, vigor, glory and heritage alive. BILL HORN County Supervisor 5th District
Numerous comparisons have been made between SDSU and other schools with Indian mascots.  But SDSU is the only school to name a specific leader as the mascot.  Although I am somewhat ambivalent about the use of Aztecs as a nickname, I do feel regret over the flippant treatment of the noted leader Montezuma.
It is the statue of Montezuma that is objectionable.  Carved in 1937 by the famous artist Donal Hord, it has been widely praised for its artistic quality.  The artist originally named it "The Aztec," but the statue later assumed the label of Montezuma.  This was unfortunate because the facial features, particularly the nose and ears, have been exaggerated, possibly as a concession to the statue's role as a mascot.  It is doubtful that it bears any likeness to the subject.  I could understand why some native Aztecs might take offense.
Although the statue is a fine work of art in its own way, it has outlived its usefulness as "Montezuma." Perhaps this would be a good time to retire it, change it back to "The Aztec," or rename it "The San Diego State Aztec." MARC B. LOGAN San Diego
We support keeping the Aztec name and images as they stand, and find no grounds to consider them offensive or derogatory.  As a matter of fact, quite the opposite.
However, in the event of a decision to change, may we suggest a slot machine as mascot and logo.
This is, indeed, a symbol of cultural unity that would offend neither the dominant Anglo population nor the Native Americans.  They both love it.  It symbolizes the one element that has served to unify these former adversaries: money!  The two cultures appear to find self-aggrandizement honorable and respectable.  Certainly, in a capitalist society, students can aim no higher.
"San Diego State Slots" has a nice ring to it -- something all Native American students would support. MARK and RACHEL ALDRICH La Mesa

Getting and keeping high-tech workers in labor-short California
The San Diego Union-Tribune
November 22, 2000, Wednesday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION;Pg. B-7; OP ED COLUMN.
Length: 849 words
Byline: Julian Betts; Betts is a senior fellow at the Public Policy Institute of California and an associate professor at UCSD.
Body

Following its overwhelming approval in Congress last month, President Clinton has signed into law a bill that will increase the number of temporary work visas INS officials can grant to skilled foreigners each year.
A majority of workers holding the "specialty occupation" H-1B visas are in the high-tech field.  The measure was a leading priority of tech executives across California who say the pool of qualified workers in the United States is simply not big enough to meet demand.
Increasing the number of visas granted to skilled foreigners may keep California's labor squeeze at bay for now.  But, by itself, visa expansion is no long-term solution.
In reality, the key pieces of California's skilled worker puzzle lie right at home.  California's colleges produced only about half the degrees needed by the state's labor market between 1970 and 1990 and imported the other half from out of state.  Such high levels of dependence on outside sources for skilled labor could have serious implications: California's high-tech industry is seeing growing competition from other areas of the country and other parts of the world, and the state is facing some serious quality-of-life issues that could deter skilled people from coming here.
One way to lower the risk of a future labor shortage is for California to commit itself to producing a larger share of its skilled work force.
A clear policy response is to expand support for the state's university and college system.  By increasing the capacity of the postsecondary school system to produce skilled labor, California will lower its reliance on other states and countries and increase the ability of young Californians to participate in rewarding occupations.
Encouragingly, significant strides have already been made in this area: The recent expansion of the CalGrant program, which guarantees tuition grant money to any student who meets grade and financial need requirements, is the boldest step this state has ever taken to increase college attendance among our youth.
At the same time that measures are taken to increase the number of young people who have access to higher education in California, the state must also take steps to ensure that more public university students enter high-tech majors.  It is a little-known fact that the cost of educating students in high-tech fields such as engineering and science often exceeds the student fees and state funding a campus receives.  Per-student subsidies from the state simply do not reflect the significantly higher costs of training students in these majors.  As a result, every single campus in the University of California system currently limits enrollment in "impacted majors" such as computer science.  In fact, seven out of eight UC campuses serving undergraduates have set enrollment limits for engineering and/or computer science, and six of eight campuses have set limits on biological sciences.
How sadly ironic that during a time of immense high-tech worker demand, California college students are regularly denied entry to high-tech majors due to rigid funding mechanisms.  The state should closely re-examine the level of support it provides the UC and Cal State systems so that any qualified student who wants to can enter these important fields of study.
In addition to expanding educational opportunities, we need to make meaningful efforts to preserve California's quality of life -- something we are seriously compromising.  Even if California begins to produce a healthier proportion of its skilled labor force, we will continue to need significant numbers of workers from other states and countries to support our economy.
How long can California continue to attract and retain highly skilled and educated workers despite soaring housing prices, agonizing traffic congestion, and alarming spikes in power prices?  Some of our most skilled workers come from other parts of the country.  To continue to attract these American workers, we must ensure that California cities like San Diego and San Jose remain as appealing places to live as Seattle, Arlington, Va. or Austin, Texas.
Additionally, although many foreigners have a strong desire to come to the United States and may focus less on quality of life issues, we shouldn't assume that the United States is the only choice these skilled workers have.  European countries like Germany and France, which are looking toward easing their rules on foreign workers, could become more tempting destinations.
Of course, the maddening irony in all this is that the thing we need -- more skilled labor -- is the very same thing fueling the population boom that threatens our quality of life.  The answer may be a three-pronged approach: a continued commitment to increasing temporary work visas for foreign workers, a dedicated effort to expanding the state's postsecondary educational systems to produce more of our own skilled labor force and a firm commitment to preserve our quality of life.
PPIC recently published his report "The Changing Role of Education in California's Labor Market," which is available at www.ppic.org.

Industry: PASSPORTS & VISAS (94%); EMPLOYMENT VISAS (90%); COLLEGES & UNIVERSITIES (89%); PUBLIC SCHOOLS (88%); EDUCATIONAL SERVICES (77%); EDUCATION SYSTEMS & INSTITUTIONS (76%); INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY INDUSTRY (73%); COMPUTER SCIENCE (63%)

Imported know-how; When U.S. firms need help, they turn to India's high-tech hiring hall
The San Diego Union-Tribune
July 16, 2000, Sunday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. I-1; STATISTICS.
Length: 1229 words
Byline: Dean Calbreath; STAFF WRITER
Body

Silicon Raj. That's Hindi for Silicon Kingdom.  It's what some wags call Silicon Valley these days -- a nod to the 200,000 or so Indians working in the cradle of American technology.
Over the last few years, Indian entrepreneurs have helped spawn such firms as Cirrus Logic, Sun Microsystems and Hotmail.  Indian engineers have filled the laboratories of IBM, Hewlett-Packard and Microsoft.
Due to their success in the high-tech arena, Fortune magazine recently rated Indians as America's most successful immigrants, since they have created companies worth a total of $235 billion.
"Indian entrepreneurs are doing very well in India and Silicon Valley," India's finance minister, Yashwant Sinha, said during a recent visit to Stanford.
But if San Jose is becoming Silicon Raj, San Diego County might someday become Wireless Raj, thanks to the growing number of Indians employed here.
One indication of the community's size came last month when The Indus Entrepreneur, an international Indian business association, inaugurated its local chapter.  The association drew about 300 executives, engineers and scientists to a meeting hall in La Jolla, far exceeding the organizers' expectations.
Indians have a firm footing in the area's high-tech firms, thanks partly to H-1B visas, which allow companies to import high-tech workers from overseas.  An estimated 300-400 Indian engineers work at Qualcomm.  Large contingents of Indians work in other labs.
The ranks are expected to swell if Congress expands the H-1B program.  Currently, Indians make up the largest number of H-1Bs, or 46 percent of the 650,000 or so H-1Bs working in the United States.
In a bill before Congress, the annual allotment of H-1Bs could increase from 115,000 to 200,000 per year, which could result in a proportionate number of new Indian emigres.
"Percentage-wise, there's probably an equal ratio of tech people in India and America, but since India has nearly a billion people, the number gets significant," says Raghu Sugavanam, founder of IndoPortals.Com in La Jolla.  The company creates Web sites for Indian computer users.
Last year, 183,000 high-tech engineers graduated from India's 1,230 technical colleges and universities.  Since opportunities are bleak in India -- the country has between 165,000 and 250,000 software positions -- the graduates look for work outside the country.
"From some schools, 60 percent of computer science or engineering graduates come to the United States," says Baljit Gai, a founder of The Indus Entrepreneur, or TIE chapter.  "Getting a degree from the Indian Institute of Technology (India's version of MIT) is like getting a green card."
Although the recent boom in Indian-owned businesses has thrown a spotlight on the Indian community, the immigration process is not new.
Business strategist C.K. Prahalad, who coined the phrase "core competencies," notes that the first wave arrived 30 years ago, when such Fortune 500 companies as General Motors and Ford began tapping the Indian Institute of Technology, or IIT, for engineering talent.
That wave of immigrants gradually landed leading positions in engineering schools or high-tech companies.
Prahalad, who recently bought a house in Rancho Santa Fe, was among the first wave, arriving in the United States in the early 1970s to study at Harvard, then to teach at the University of Michigan at Ann Arbor.
Baljit Gai, Raghu Sugavanam and Prahalad's business partner, Ramesh Jain, were among the Indians who moved to the United States during the same period.
A former professor at IIT, Jain is one of San Diego's most successful Indian entrepreneurs.  Jain came to this country to teach electrical and computer engineering, first at Ann Arbor and then at the University of California San Diego.
As a pioneer in artificial intelligence, Jain shifted easily from academia to the business world.  In 1990, he launched Imageware, specializing in virtual modeling and reverse engineering.  After selling Imageware, he created Virage, which develops visual-information systems.
Although Virage is now based in San Mateo, Jain launched it from his home in San Diego, assisted by five UCSD students.  When it went public last month, its stock jumped from an opening of $11 per share to the $20 range.
Jain's current project is Praja, which offers such products as a program that lets sports fans construct their own football highlights on the computer.
Prahalad, chairman of Praja, says Jain's story is typical of that first wave of Indian immigrants.  "They established a good base, and now we're seeing the next generation build on that strength," he says.
In fact, Praja is giving other Indians a boost by hiring Indian engineers.  About 10 of Praja's 60 employees in Sorrento Valley come from India, thanks in part to the H-1B visa.  Jain says that without such visas, he would be hard-pressed to find skilled workers in San Diego County, where the jobless rate for engineers hovers around 1 or 2 percent.
Baljit Gai, a TIE executive, sees a downside to such massive immigration, however.  "India spends an enormous amount of money to educate people and once they get the education, they just leave," he sighs.
Not everyone in the Indian community shares that view.
Paula Chakravartty, assistant professor of communication at UCSD, notes that Indian emigres send billions of dollars to their homeland each year.  C.K. Prahalad adds that the chief benefit to India is brainpower.
"If only a small portion of the knowledge being accrued in the United States trickles back to India, it's better than any money," Prahalad says.  "You can expect to see a lot of products being developed in India."
Many of those products may be developed under the auspices of Indian-owned companies in the United States.
Praja, for instance, recently signed a contract with Complete Business Solutions India to handle some of its programming at a 300-worker plant in Bangalore, India's version of Silicon Valley.
IndoPortals maintains a work crew in Bangalore.  Burning Glass, which develops artificial-intelligence programs for the employment industry, has a small team in India handling much of its data inputting.
"The 12-hour time difference allows them to work while we're asleep," says Krishna Gopinathan, the co-founder of Burning Glass and a Canadian of Indian descent.
Of course, a key attraction in India is the low pay scale.  Engineers that make $100,000 in the United States might be paid $10,000 in India, which draws such firms as Motorola and Ericsson to its shores.
Supporters of the H-1B program wield India's low salaries as a club against critics, saying that if companies cannot bring foreign workers to the United States, they will hire them at a much lower rate in their native lands.
But the shift to India has already begun.  Thanks largely to contracts from foreign companies, software exports from India are increasing 60 percent per year.  This year, India is projected to export $3.9 billion in software, with 60 percent headed for the United States.
Technology already is changing India.  Family farmers are selling milk via the Internet.  In the cities, people are ordering movie tickets or buying pizza over the Web. Though only 1 in 100 people can afford a computer, they can rent time from somebody who has one.
"There's a tremendous amount of feeling in India that computers will deliver people from poverty to riches," Prahalad says.

Geographic: SAN DIEGO, CA, USA (94%); SILICON VALLEY, CA, USA (92%); SAN FRANCISCO BAY AREA, CA, USA (90%); SAN JOSE, CA, USA (73%); INDIA (95%); UNITED STATES (94%)

Getting and keeping high-tech workers in labor-short California
The San Diego Union-Tribune
November 22, 2000 Wednesday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. B-7

At the same time that measures are taken to increase the number of young people who have access to higher education in California, the state must also take steps to ensure that more public university students enter high-tech majors.
It is a little-known fact that the cost of educating students in high-tech fields such as engineering and science often exceeds the student fees and state funding a campus receives.
Per-student subsidies from the state simply do not reflect the significantly higher costs of training students in these majors.
As a result, every single campus in the University of California system currently limits enrollment in "impacted majors" such as computer science.
In fact, seven out of eight UC campuses serving undergraduates have set enrollment limits for engineering and/or computer science, and six of eight campuses have set limits on biological sciences. How sadly ironic that during a time of immense high-tech worker demand, California college students are regularly denied entry to high-tech majors due to rigid funding mechanisms.
The state should closely re-examine the level of support it provides the UC and Cal State systems so that any qualified student who wants to can enter these important fields of study. In addition to expanding educational opportunities, we need to make meaningful efforts to preserve California's quality of life -- something we are seriously compromising.
Even if California begins to produce a healthier proportion of its skilled labor force, we will continue to need significant numbers of workers from other states and countries to support our economy. How long can California continue to attract and retain highly skilled and educated workers despite soaring housing prices, agonizing traffic congestion, and alarming spikes in power prices?
Some of our most skilled workers come from other parts of the country.
To continue to attract these American workers, we must ensure that California cities like San Diego and San Jose remain as appealing places to live as Seattle, Arlington, Va. or Austin, Texas. Additionally, although many foreigners have a strong desire to come to the United States and may focus less on quality of life issues, we shouldn't assume that the United States is the only choice these skilled workers have.
European countries like Germany and France, which are looking toward easing their rules on foreign workers, could become more tempting destinations. Of course, the maddening irony in all this is that the thing we need -- more skilled labor -- is the very same thing fueling the population boom that threatens our quality of life.
The answer may be a three-pronged approach: a continued commitment to increasing temporary work visas for foreign workers, a dedicated effort to expanding the state's postsecondary educational systems to produce more of our own skilled labor force and a firm commitment to preserve our quality of life. PPIC recently published his report "The Changing Role of Education in California's Labor Market," which is available at www.ppic.org.

Imported know-how; When U.S. firms need help, they turn to India's high-tech hiring hall
The San Diego Union-Tribune
July 16, 2000 Sunday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS; Pg. I-1
Length: 1228 words
Byline: Dean Calbreath, STAFF WRITER
Body


Silicon Raj. That's Hindi for Silicon Kingdom.
It's what some wags call Silicon Valley these days -- a nod to the 200,000 or so Indians working in the cradle of American technology. Over the last few years, Indian entrepreneurs have helped spawn such firms as Cirrus Logic, Sun Microsystems and Hotmail.
Indian engineers have filled the laboratories of IBM, Hewlett-Packard and Microsoft. Due to their success in the high-tech arena, Fortune magazine recently rated Indians as America's most successful immigrants, since they have created companies worth a total of $235 billion. "Indian entrepreneurs are doing very well in India and Silicon Valley," India's finance minister, Yashwant Sinha, said during a recent visit to Stanford. But if San Jose is becoming Silicon Raj, San Diego County might someday become Wireless Raj, thanks to the growing number of Indians employed here. One indication of the community's size came last month when The Indus Entrepreneur, an international Indian business association, inaugurated its local chapter.
The association drew about 300 executives, engineers and scientists to a meeting hall in La Jolla, far exceeding the organizers' expectations. Indians have a firm footing in the area's high-tech firms, thanks partly to H-1B visas, which allow companies to import high-tech workers from overseas.
An estimated 300-400 Indian engineers work at Qualcomm.
Large contingents of Indians work in other labs. The ranks are expected to swell if Congress expands the H-1B program. Currently, Indians make up the largest number of H-1Bs, or 46 percent of the 650,000 or so H-1Bs working in the United States. In a bill before Congress, the annual allotment of H-1Bs could increase from 115,000 to 200,000 per year, which could result in a proportionate number of new Indian emigres. "Percentage-wise, there's probably an equal ratio of tech people in India and America, but since India has nearly a billion people, the number gets significant," says Raghu Sugavanam, founder of IndoPortals.Com in La Jolla. The company creates Web sites for Indian computer users. Last year, 183,000 high-tech engineers graduated from India's 1,230 technical colleges and universities.
Since opportunities are bleak in India -- the country has between 165,000 and 250,000 software positions -- the graduates look for work outside the country. "From some schools, 60 percent of computer science or engineering graduates come to the United States," says Baljit Gai, a founder of The Indus Entrepreneur, or TIE chapter.
"Getting a degree from the Indian Institute of Technology (India's version of MIT) is like getting a green card." Although the recent boom in Indian-owned businesses has thrown a spotlight on the Indian community, the immigration process is not new. Business strategist C.K. Prahalad, who coined the phrase "core competencies," notes that the first wave arrived 30 years ago, when such Fortune 500 companies as General Motors and Ford began tapping the Indian Institute of Technology, or IIT, for engineering talent. That wave of immigrants gradually landed leading positions in engineering schools or high-tech companies. Prahalad, who recently bought a house in Rancho Santa Fe, was among the first wave, arriving in the United States in the early 1970s to study at Harvard, then to teach at the University of Michigan at Ann Arbor. Baljit Gai, Raghu Sugavanam and Prahalad's business partner, Ramesh Jain, were among the Indians who moved to the United States during the same period. A former professor at IIT, Jain is one of San Diego's most successful Indian entrepreneurs.
Jain came to this country to teach electrical and computer engineering, first at Ann Arbor and then at the University of California San Diego. As a pioneer in artificial intelligence, Jain shifted easily from academia to the business world.
In 1990, he launched Imageware, specializing in virtual modeling and reverse engineering.
After selling Imageware, he created Virage, which develops visual-information systems. Although Virage is now based in San Mateo, Jain launched it from his home in San Diego, assisted by five UCSD students.
When it went public last month, its stock jumped from an opening of $11 per share to the $20 range. Jain's current project is Praja, which offers such products as a program that lets sports fans construct their own football highlights on the computer. Prahalad, chairman of Praja, says Jain's story is typical of that first wave of Indian immigrants.
"They established a good base, and now we're seeing the next generation build on that strength," he says. In fact, Praja is giving other Indians a boost by hiring Indian engineers. About 10 of Praja's 60 employees in Sorrento Valley come from India, thanks in part to the H-1B visa.
Jain says that without such visas, he would be hard-pressed to find skilled workers in San Diego County, where the jobless rate for engineers hovers around 1 or 2 percent. Baljit Gai, a TIE executive, sees a downside to such massive immigration, however.
"India spends an enormous amount of money to educate people and once they get the education, they just leave," he sighs. Not everyone in the Indian community shares that view. Paula Chakravartty, assistant professor of communication at UCSD, notes that Indian emigres send billions of dollars to their homeland each year. C.K. Prahalad adds that the chief benefit to India is brainpower. "If only a small portion of the knowledge being accrued in the United States trickles back to India, it's better than any money," Prahalad says. "You can expect to see a lot of products being developed in India." Many of those products may be developed under the auspices of Indian-owned companies in the United States. Praja, for instance, recently signed a contract with Complete Business Solutions India to handle some of its programming at a 300-worker plant in Bangalore, India's version of Silicon Valley. IndoPortals maintains a work crew in Bangalore.
Burning Glass, which develops artificial-intelligence programs for the employment industry, has a small team in India handling much of its data inputting. "The 12-hour time difference allows them to work while we're asleep," says Krishna Gopinathan, the co-founder of Burning Glass and a Canadian of Indian descent. Of course, a key attraction in India is the low pay scale.
Engineers that make $100,000 in the United States might be paid $10,000 in India, which draws such firms as Motorola and Ericsson to its shores. Supporters of the H-1B program wield India's low salaries as a club against critics, saying that if companies cannot bring foreign workers to the United States, they will hire them at a much lower rate in their native lands. But the shift to India has already begun.
Thanks largely to contracts from foreign companies, software exports from India are increasing 60 percent per year.
This year, India is projected to export $3.9 billion in software, with 60 percent headed for the United States. Technology already is changing India.
Family farmers are selling milk via the Internet.
In the cities, people are ordering movie tickets or buying pizza over the Web. Though only 1 in 100 people can afford a computer, they can rent time from somebody who has one. "There's a tremendous amount of feeling in India that computers will deliver people from poverty to riches," Prahalad says.

SUPPLY AND DEMAND; For San Diego businesses, tight labor market puts a premium on hiring and keeping help
The San Diego Union-Tribune
December 25, 2000, Monday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. D-1; STATISTICS. WRAP.
Length: 923 words
Byline: Michael Kinsman; staff writer
Body

Last summer, the owner of a small hotel in North County was complaining about not being able to hire a front-desk clerk.
He had been paying $6.50 an hour a year previously and had no trouble filling the job.  Now he was advertising the same job for $10 an hour.  His phone hardly rang at all.
"It's frustrating," he says.  "I don't want to pay someone $12 or $13 an hour for this job, but it might take that to get someone good."
Welcome to the world of work in San Diego County in 2000. Labor is scarce, and experienced, competent people who are looking for work know that.
And, although companies have been slow to acknowledge it, the message is finally getting through that good workers are at a premium.
Unemployment in San Diego County likely will end 2000 right at the 3 percent mark, according to the state Employment Development Department.  That's a historic low for the region and less than half what it was in 1995.
That shrinking pool of available labor seems to be having an impact throughout the economy.
John DeWitt, vice president of Lee Hecht Harrison, a career services firm, says the region's employers have pushed hiring up a couple of notches on their to-do lists.
"There seems to be a realization that hiring is more difficult," he says, and since firms can't do a good job without the right people, they had "better pay more attention to how (they) handle that." That realization has streamlined some recruiting and hiring processes.
"We're seeing major corporations making more and more hiring decisions the day people come in to interview," DeWitt says.
Concerned that there are other jobs waiting for good workers, some companies will tender a job offer on the spot.
"Resumes now get looked at the moment they come in," DeWitt says.  "If a company is interested, (it will) call that person to schedule an interview within 24 hours.  This seems to have come from the realization that a good person is going to have multiple job opportunities."
Unemployment hasn't reached 4 percent in more than three years in San Diego County, creating a sustained competition for talent that ripples through all levels of the job market.
Nowhere is that more evident than in the high-tech sector.  Throughout the 1990s, the growth of technology companies has propelled the region's economy.
But, strapped by a shortage of qualified technical workers and a rapidly expanding field, high-tech firms are feeling the employment pinch more than many other companies. needs
In San Diego County, technology companies estimate the unemployment rate for engineers is less than 1 percent, with some of those holding outdated skills.
That has strained plans for growth at many companies and fueled a debate over allowing more work visas for foreign workers under the federal government's H-1B program.
One report estimated that from 1970 to 1990, California universities and colleges awarded only half the degrees necessary for the state's burgeoning high-tech work force.  That inability to keep up with the demands of business meant California companies either had to import foreign workers or slow down growth.
This fall, President Clinton signed legislation to admit 195,000 foreign workers annually for the next three years under the H-1B visa program.
In fiscal 2000, the United States already had granted 115,000 such visas and without the legislation that number was to have declined over the next few years.
The tight labor market is reaching into other sectors, too.
Phil Blair, co-owner of Manpower Temporary Services, says a manufacturer raised pay for temporary assembly-line help this fall from $6.25 an hour to $7 and is considering an increase to $7.50 early in 2001.
"This employer placed a large order with us and I couldn't do it," Blair says.  "I told them that these workers could get more from other employers, and I couldn't in good conscience steer them to the lower-paying company." wage gains
In October, the state agreed to raise its minimum wage 50 cents to $6.25 an hour on Jan. 1. That will be followed with an additional 50 cents an hour on Jan. 1, 2002. The increase in minimum wage is expected to affect about 1 million Californians.
Blair is aware of what a dramatic bump in pay schedules could mean:
The $7 an hour worker will want $8, the $10 an hour worker will want $12 and so on.  "You see wages go up like this, and you know that inflation can't be far behind," he says.
Meanwhile, in the rush to fill jobs, some employers are reviewing their hiring criteria.
A company might now accept six months of experience, rather than hold out for a year, or maybe it will be satisfied with someone's ability to take directions in English rather than possess fluency in English.
"Employers seem willing to re-think what they are demanding these days," Blair says.
For now, large salary increases and signing bonuses are being offered for many key jobs.  In addition, some firms employing temporary workers are offering attendance bonuses of $50 for not missing a single workday over a month, or $250 for not missing a day of work during the quarter.
"Keeping good employees in this market is as big an issue as finding them," DeWitt says.  "If you lose two good people along the way, have you really gained anything?
"You don't want to lose a good worker if you're not sure you can replace them.  That's why at the professional level we see a lot of people being offered counteroffers to remain with their company.  Companies know that it's financially beneficial to hand out a raise rather than go looking for another difficult-to-find worker."

2 DRAWINGS; 1. Tim Teebken / Artville

Company: LEE HECHT HARRISON INC  (55%); LEE HECHT HARRISON INC  (55%)

Industry: HOTEL STAFF (90%); HOTELS & MOTELS (73%)

Geographic: CALIFORNIA, USA (94%)

Does America really need even more foreign workers?
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 7, 2000 Thursday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. B-11
Length: 808 words
Byline: Byron Slater, Slater is a member of the Border Solution Task Force, an immigration control group.
Body


Almost every employer seems to be clamoring for temporary foreign workers these days.
In fact, many high-tech computer and electronics firms have been pressuring Congress to increase the quota of H1B visas that would allow foreign workers to come to the U.S. for up to three years. Under this arrangement, the employer pays a fee and then is allowed to hire an engineer or programmer or some other skilled worker, from out of the country, to work in their company for a limited time. Here's the rub.
They often replace existing workers at reduced wages, and in many cases, do not hire older or newly graduated, prospective employees. This practice has done wonders for the corporate bottom line and these foreign workers are literally chained to their desk.
In many cases, they are forced to work long hours without overtime pay and usually do not receive benefits offered to permanent employees. In addition, they are forced to stay with one employer for up to three years, without the ability to shop for a better-paying job.
It's the nearest thing to involuntary servitude. Is there a dire need for these foreign high-tech workers?
Not according to Norman Matloff, professor of computer technology at University of California at Davis.
Matloff has been tracking high-tech computer jobs for years, and he has repeatedly decried the so-called dire need for these foreign workers. According to Matloff, there are ample qualified applicants for most of the jobs filled by the foreign workers.
He cites a bumper crop of newly graduated engineers and many seasoned workers over 40 who are having difficulty landing jobs in the youth-oriented computer industry.
Matloff also points to the fact that many high-tech and dot-com companies are facing tough times in the current market and are unable to increase their work force in a slowing market. There are several bills before Congress that would increase the current limit of 115,000 visas to as many as 200,000. In fact, one bill offered by Rep. Lamar Smith, R-Texas, the chairman of the immigration subcommittee, has been approved by that committee.
It would offer an unlimited number of potential foreign workers.
The industry favors another bill co-authored by Reps. David Dreier, R-Covina and Zoe Lofrgen, D-San Jose, that would allow nearly 200,000 workers.
They prefer this bill over the open-ended Smith bill because it does not provide the job protection for American workers, required under Smith's bill. On top of the hundreds of thousands of foreign workers for high-tech industries, farmers are clamoring for more field hands under a similar plan that is fashioned after the bracero program, which was ended in the 1960s. It was opposed by the labor unions and many pro-immigrant groups, due to poor treatment of the workers and barely-habitable housing conditions.
The net result of the bracero program was that huge numbers of illegal immigrants decided to remain in the United States.
This created a demand for the amnesty of 1986, in which 3.1 million illegal immigrants received legal status. The law provided stiff penalties for employers who knowingly hired illegal immigrants. It was also supposed to beef up the border to stop more illegals from entering the country.
The problem was that the law was not enforced until immigration reform legislation passed in 1996. In addition to the demand for foreign workers, the Clinton administration wants to add an amnesty for up to 500,000 illegal immigrants now living in the United States.
This amnesty would cover Central Americans who came to this country during the war in El Salvador, Guatemala and Haiti under asylum laws. The problem is that once they got here, they refused to go home at the end of the war.
This is contrary to our immigration laws and would allow them to circumvent the long wait for legal entry. Therein lies the problem.
If we give amnesty to this group, it would be a signal to the impoverished around the world that if you can get across our border, you're home free and will be given legal status sooner or later.
It would also penalize those who have patiently waited for legal entry.
When the U.S. government granted amnesty in 1986, it was supposed to stop illegal immigration. In fact, the exact opposite has resulted.
Today, the Immigration and Naturalization Service admits that 6 million illegal aliens are residing in the country.
Some say the figure is as high 10 million or more. If the Clinton administration and pro-business Republicans get their way, we might as well scrap any semblance of an effective immigration policy. The H1B program must be carefully administered to prevent the fraud that has been documented in the past, and there cannot be a general amnesty if America is to retain any semblance of control over its borders.
America must control immigration in order to slow the runaway population explosion.

Language: ENGLISH

Document-Type: OP ED COLUMN; 

Publication-Type: Newspaper

Subject: EMPLOYMENT VISAS (90%); FOREIGN LABOR (90%); FARM LABOR (89%); IMMIGRATION (89%); OLDER WORKERS (89%); PASSPORTS & VISAS (89%); TECHNICIANS & TECHNOLOGICAL WORKERS (89%); COMPENSATION & BENEFITS (79%); LABOR FORCE (79%); LABOR UNIONS (79%); OVERTIME (79%); TEMPORARY EMPLOYMENT (79%); WAGES & SALARIES (79%); WORKFORCE DEMAND (79%); US REPUBLICAN PARTY (78%); APPROVALS (72%); COLLEGES & UNIVERSITIES (66%); EDITORIALS & OPINIONS (59%); REPORTS, REVIEWS & SECTIONS (59%); COLLEGE & UNIVERSITY PROFESSORS (51%); EMPLOYMENT (%); IMMIGRATION (%); LAW (%); SALARIES (%); USA (%)

Organization: UNIVERSITY OF CALIFORNIA (55%)

Industry: ELECTRONICS (90%); EMPLOYMENT VISAS (90%); FARM LABOR (89%); PASSPORTS & VISAS (89%); COMPUTER EQUIPMENT (78%); COMPUTING & INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (78%); INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY INDUSTRY (78%); COLLEGES & UNIVERSITIES (66%); COLLEGE & UNIVERSITY PROFESSORS (51%)

Person: LAMAR SMITH (77%); DAVID DREIER (58%)

Geographic: SAN DIEGO, CA, USA (93%); SAN JOSE, CA, USA (79%); CALIFORNIA, USA (79%); TEXAS, USA (79%); UNITED STATES (94%)

SUPPLY AND DEMAND; For San Diego businesses, tight labor market puts a premium on hiring and keeping help
The San Diego Union-Tribune
December 25, 2000 Monday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS; Pg. D-1
Length: 924 words
Byline: Michael Kinsman, staff writer
Body

In addition, some firms employing temporary workers are offering attendance bonuses of $50 for not missing a single workday over a month, or $250 for not missing a day of work during the quarter. "Keeping good employees in this market is as big an issue as finding them," DeWitt says.
"If you lose two good people along the way, have you really gained anything? "You don't want to lose a good worker if you're not sure you can replace them.
That's why at the professional level we see a lot of people being offered counteroffers to remain with their company.
Companies know that it's financially beneficial to hand out a raise rather than go looking for another difficult-to-find worker."

Does America really need even more foreign workers?
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 7, 2000, Thursday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION;Pg. B-11; OP ED COLUMN.
Length: 814 words
Byline: Byron Slater; Slater is a member of the Border Solution Task Force, an immigration control group.
Body

Almost every employer seems to be clamoring for temporary foreign workers these days.  In fact, many high-tech computer and electronics firms have been pressuring Congress to increase the quota of H1B visas that would allow foreign workers to come to the U.S. for up to three years.
Under this arrangement, the employer pays a fee and then is allowed to hire an engineer or programmer or some other skilled worker, from out of the country, to work in their company for a limited time.
Here's the rub.  They often replace existing workers at reduced wages, and in many cases, do not hire older or newly graduated, prospective employees.  This practice has done wonders for the corporate bottom line and these foreign workers are literally chained to their desk.  In many cases, they are forced to work long hours without overtime pay and usually do not receive benefits offered to permanent employees.
In addition, they are forced to stay with one employer for up to three years, without the ability to shop for a better-paying job.  It's the nearest thing to involuntary servitude.
Is there a dire need for these foreign high-tech workers?  Not according to Norman Matloff, professor of computer technology at University of California at Davis.  Matloff has been tracking high-tech computer jobs for years, and he has repeatedly decried the so-called dire need for these foreign workers.
According to Matloff, there are ample qualified applicants for most of the jobs filled by the foreign workers.  He cites a bumper crop of newly graduated engineers and many seasoned workers over 40 who are having difficulty landing jobs in the youth-oriented computer industry.  Matloff also points to the fact that many high-tech and dot-com companies are facing tough times in the current market and are unable to increase their work force in a slowing market.
There are several bills before Congress that would increase the current limit of 115,000 visas to as many as 200,000. In fact, one bill offered by Rep. Lamar Smith, R-Texas, the chairman of the immigration subcommittee, has been approved by that committee.  It would offer an unlimited number of potential foreign workers.  The industry favors another bill co-authored by Reps. David Dreier, R-Covina and Zoe Lofrgen, D-San Jose, that would allow nearly 200,000 workers.  They prefer this bill over the open-ended Smith bill because it does not provide the job protection for American workers, required under Smith's bill.
On top of the hundreds of thousands of foreign workers for high-tech industries, farmers are clamoring for more field hands under a similar plan that is fashioned after the bracero program, which was ended in the 1960s. It was opposed by the labor unions and many pro-immigrant groups, due to poor treatment of the workers and barely-habitable housing conditions.  The net result of the bracero program was that huge numbers of illegal immigrants decided to remain in the United States.  This created a demand for the amnesty of 1986, in which 3.1 million illegal immigrants received legal status.
The law provided stiff penalties for employers who knowingly hired illegal immigrants.
It was also supposed to beef up the border to stop more illegals from entering the country.  The problem was that the law was not enforced until immigration reform legislation passed in 1996.
In addition to the demand for foreign workers, the Clinton administration wants to add an amnesty for up to 500,000 illegal immigrants now living in the United States.  This amnesty would cover Central Americans who came to this country during the war in El Salvador, Guatemala and Haiti under asylum laws.
The problem is that once they got here, they refused to go home at the end of the war.  This is contrary to our immigration laws and would allow them to circumvent the long wait for legal entry.
Therein lies the problem.  If we give amnesty to this group, it would be a signal to the impoverished around the world that if you can get across our border, you're home free and will be given legal status sooner or later.  It would also penalize those who have patiently waited for legal entry.  When the U.S. government granted amnesty in 1986, it was supposed to stop illegal immigration.
In fact, the exact opposite has resulted.  Today, the Immigration and Naturalization Service admits that 6 million illegal aliens are residing in the country.  Some say the figure is as high 10 million or more.
If the Clinton administration and pro-business Republicans get their way, we might as well scrap any semblance of an effective immigration policy.  The H1B program must be carefully administered to prevent the fraud that has been documented in the past, and there cannot be a general amnesty if America is to retain any semblance of control over its borders.  America must control immigration in order to slow the runaway population explosion.

Entrepreneurs, e-policy and people
The San Diego Union-Tribune
February 1, 2001 Thursday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. B-9:2,6,7; B-7:1
Length: 732 words
Byline: J.R. Beyster, Beyster is chairman, president and chief executive officer of Science Applications International Corporation (SAIC), a Fortune 500 high-technology firm, and is a commissioner of the National Commission on Entrepreneurship.
Body


The 2000 presidential election will go down in history as a time when razor-thin margins tested antiquated voting systems, our judicial process, the U.S. Constitution and, most certainly, the patience of the American people.
For some entrepreneurs in the New Economy, the elections also signaled a growing trend in which political candidates staked a claim to the high-tech industry and promised to help it prosper. Candidates in both parties helped to advance some issues for the New Economy in the last Congress and have pledged more support over the next few years.
For the most part, however, they have focused on the "e" (as in electronic) when they should be focusing more on the "p" (as in people). While taxation of the Internet and the binding effect of electronic signatures are certainly important public policy issues, the most critical issue facing entrepreneurs is more profound.
It is whether America's entrepreneurs can find the people -- technicians, salespeople and managers -- to build and grow new companies and create jobs. The National Commission on Entrepreneurship recently asked 250 entrepreneurs to identify the primary challenge to starting and growing a new company.
Without exception, finding and keeping good people ranked at the top of the list.
Te participants in the study came from both dot-com and "bricks and mortar" companies, showing that the struggle to hire capable employees affects WalMart and Seven-Eleven as much as high-tech companies.
In addition, a group of San Diego entrepreneurs who participated in the study said the shortage of quality workers has driven up both salaries and benefits. The 106th Congress did take an important step forward when, at the end of the session, it passed legislation to raise the cap on H-1B visas.
These temporary visas for highly skilled foreign workers are a critical component to solving the worker shortage. Both the Congress and the outgoing president are to be congratulated for addressing this pressing issue for entrepreneurs and for the economy.
Yet the 200,000 employees who will enter the country under this program will make only a small dent in the problem. With so many companies scrambling for the same talent, it is likely that many jobs for skilled high-tech workers will go unfilled.
Right now SAIC's telecommunications subsidiary Telcordia Technologies has 1,400 unfilled positions available, and it is unlikely that Telcordia will be able to fill these jobs from the small H-1B visa pool. The work-force shortage is not limited to skilled workers.
There also is a shortage of entry-level workers who have the communications and problem-solving skills necessary to serve customers and conduct basic business tasks.
Entrepreneurial companies can train new employees in specific technologies for their businesses, but they are not equipped to provide remedial education in fundamental skills.
Educational institutions, government, and entrepreneurs should work together to prepare more young people for all types of jobs -- many of which are increasingly technical. The war for talent, however, cannot be won by better education alone.
It also is important to provide incentives for employees to want to stay with an organization. Programs such as telework, where employees spend some amount of time working remotely, can be a boon to employee morale and retention.
Studies indicate that telework programs are responsible for a 20 percent increase in employee productivity. Also, revised tax laws could help firms expand employee ownership through stock options.
There are a few proposals brought before the last Congress, and certain to be reintroduced, that would create a "super stock option," combining the best of these options' advantages to both employers and employees.
These proposals should be seriously considered as ways to increase employee ownership and entrepreneurship. Most importantly, policy-makers need to recognize the role entrepreneurs play in our economy.
Their innovative products and services improve the quality of our lives, create new jobs, make the United States more competitive abroad, and help create economic growth and wealth. Our elected leaders need to enact policies that promote entrepreneurship so that economic expansion in San Diego and elsewhere across the country can continue.
We are counting on the new Congress and administration to promote our entrepreneurial economy.

Entrepreneurs, e-policy and people
The San Diego Union-Tribune
February 1, 2001, Thursday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune

A need for foreign-born professionals
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 24, 2000, Sunday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION;Pg. G-3; OP ED COLUMN.
Length: 921 words
Byline: Jeanne Malitz; Malitz is a member of the San Diego World Trade Center and attorney at law with Morrison & Foerster LLP in San Diego.
Body

The H-1B program is a prompt, lawful way for U.S. employers to hire foreign-born professionals (such as engineers and computer programmers) on a temporary basis.  This program allows U.S. businesses to recruit and hire the best-qualified candidates from around the world and compete on a level playing field with foreign companies in such key industries as telecommunications, pharmaceuticals, biotechnology and education.
The issue before Congress is whether the number of H-1B visas granted by the U.S. government should be raised from current levels, and if so, should Congress limit the use of the H-1B program by placing additional restrictions on employers and companies.
Holders of H-1B visas are foreign professionals hired on a temporary basis by U.S. employers.  They can only be hired for "specialty occupations," which are defined under the law as jobs that require a professional who has the equivalent of a bachelor's degree in their field of specialty.  Examples are doctors, engineers, professors, accountants, researchers, medical personnel and computer professionals.
The subject of H-1B visas was most recently addressed by Congress in the American Competitiveness and Workforce Improvement Act of 1998 (ACWIA). The ACWIA increased the number of H-1B visas to be granted beginning in 1999. According to the ACWIA, no more than 115,000 visas would be granted for each Fiscal Year 1999 and 2000, 107,500 in 2001, and 65,000 after that.  ACWIA also added new requirements for employers who use a higher percentage of H-1B workers, such as requiring high-use companies to first recruit in the United States, and not lay off American workers before using the H-1B program.
ACWIA also stiffened the punishments for companies that violate the law.  The new punishments include fines of up to $35,000, a three-year bar from participating in visa programs, and repaying salaries of any underpaid foreign professionals.  ACWIA further requires employers to pay a fee of $500 per visa to fund education and training programs for U.S. workers.
U.S. employers use the H-1B program to hire foreign professionals with highly needed skills.  Employers typically hire H-1B professionals for three reasons:
[] Needed skills: To obtain essential skills or rare and unique knowledge;
[] Global market expertise: For their special expertise in overseas needs, markets, trends or distribution, allowing U.S. businesses to compete in global markets;
[] Temporary shortages: To fill temporary shortages of needed skills.  If American companies are prevented from hiring essential people to fill critical positions, an increasing number of jobs dependent upon these positions will go unfilled each year, resulting in American jobs being lost and American projects losing out to foreign competition.  As the U.S. economy becomes increasingly global, H-1B professionals become ever more essential to San Diego's continued economic growth.
Larry Fitch, president and chief executive officer of San Diego Workforce Partnership, Inc., states, "The only way we will be able to permanently resolve the issue of importing workers for high technology positions is by growing our own work force.  This will require a major commitment to and investment in our youth.  Unfortunately, this is a long-term solution, and the crisis is now.  We must continue to use H-1B visas in the short term, and ensure that we are accessing the training dollars that are available with the visas to upgrade the skills of our current workers."
ACWIA increased the number of temporary visas through fiscal year 2001, while making significant changes to the program to enhance domestic work-force protections.  However, the increases mandated in ACWIA were insufficient; the H-1B visa cap was reached well before the end of Fiscal Year 1999 and was reached in March in Fiscal Year 2000.
Kim Giannone, Employment Specialist, Insight Electronics LLC says, "We firmly support an increase, and even the removal of the limit on H-1Bs. We are an international company, and we have problems filling our technical and engineering positions in all areas.  It is very difficult (if not impossible) for us to fill our openings with the available U.S. workers."
Among other things, the legislation before Congress would accomplish the following:
[] Increase the H-1B visa cap to 200,000 or more for each year for the period FY 2000-2003.
[] Exempt from the H-1B visa cap non-immigrants employed by higher educational institutions and research institutions, and foreign graduates of U.S. masters and doctoral degree programs sponsored by U.S. employers within six months of their graduation.
[] Permit extensions of H-1B status past the six-year limit for H-1B beneficiaries who have immigrant visa applications pending.
Raising the H-1B visa limitation will have an immediate and significant impact on the San Diego economy.  San Diego is one of the fastest growing regions in the country in the high-tech, biotechnology and research industries.  San Diego also is home for hundreds of start-up companies and relatively small businesses.  These industries have the greatest demand for the H-1B program.
San Diego employers use the H-1B program to obtain skilled foreign professionals at a time when unemployment is at an all-time low and competition for skilled technical workers is at an all-time high.  Increasing the H-1B visa cap without imposing restrictions on employers would greatly enhance the growth of such companies as well as encourage increased investment into the San Diego region.

1 DRAWING; Catherine Kanner
