Growing issue; Farmers see a labor crunch, field hands don't, as chances of guest-worker legislation improve
The San Diego Union-Tribune
February 23, 2001 Friday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: NEWS ; Pg. A-1
Length: 1937 words
Byline: Diane Lindquist, STAFF WRITER
Dateline: CALEXICO 
Body


CALEXICO -- By 4:30 a.m. most mornings, as many as 10,000 people are huddled on the street corners and in the parking lots of this small downtown, hoping to be hired for work in the fields. But when the employers' vans and buses pull away, scores of men and women are routinely left behind -- another day without work. This scene at the gateway to the Imperial Valley, America's winter salad bowl, happened time and again in January.
Workers far exceeded jobs, just as they routinely do in fruit- and vegetable-growing regions throughout the United States.
It's an impression bolstered by high unemployment rates in farming regions, with Imperial County's among the highest at 23 percent. Across the country in the nation's capital, however, a contradictory story is told. Farm owners are complaining to Congress of a serious labor shortage.
The only solution, they say, is a new guest-worker program that would allow them to temporarily hire foreigners. But if growers can't find enough people to harvest their fields, why are unemployment rates so high in agricultural communities?
Why is there a labor shortage amid a labor surplus? Conflicting answers to these questions have been offered for decades, as guest-worker legislation has been introduced in Congress -- and voted down. This year, however, guest-worker legislation is gaining new momentum. When President Bush and Mexican President Vicente Fox met this month, they formed a high-level team charged, among other things, with producing a guest-worker plan. Both presidents believe that such a program would help stem illegal immigration and protect Mexican workers in the United States. "The sense I've got is, this is the year if we can put the right plan together," said Bob Vice, a Fallbrook grower who as president of the National Council of Agricultural Employers leads the lobbying effort.
The organization represents three-quarters of the country's farm employers. Imperial County contract harvester Steve Scaroni points to his own situation as an example of why a guest-worker program is needed. Every day, his buses load up workers in a Calexico parking lot.
And even though thousands of people are there looking for work, he says that in the past two months his crews have been shorthanded by 5 to 50 percent. Scaroni offered explanations for why there appear to be more workers than jobs most mornings. "They probably don't want to work in the first place.
Or they're very selective about what jobs they'll do," he said.
"A lot come for the socializing, to see their friends." Scaroni also notes that field jobs, which pay slightly more or less than California's minimum wage of $6.25 an hour, aren't as attractive as other entry-level jobs, like construction. Picking fruit and vegetables is physically draining, and people quickly switch to better-paying, year-round positions. But there's little hard evidence to back up the growers' claim of a labor shortage. The California Employment Development Department "doesn't have anything that documents a labor shortage" in farm communities, said an agency spokeswoman. In fact, some of the state's highest unemployment rates in December were in agricultural regions -- the 23 percent in Imperial County, 14 percent in Fresno and Madera counties, 12 percent in Monterey County, 11 percent in Kern County, 9 percent in San Joaquin County. The national unemployment rate, meanwhile, was 4 percent. After the harvest, the rates soar even higher in agricultural regions.
In July, Imperial County's rate was 34 percent. The Employment Development Department's director, Michael Bernick, points out that many of the people collecting unemployment wouldn't be suited for field work. But he also said that California's agricultural community tends to bypass the state employment system, turning instead to informal hiring networks like the morning gatherings in downtown Calexico. Even Fallbrook grower Vice admits there's no proof of labor shortages. "But it's been tight," he adds quickly, noting that employers are going to great lengths to fill their crews.
Some offer incentives, such as paying part of the wages up front, he said.
Others accept less-qualified applicants, including those who seem drunk. Outside the Donut Avenue cafe in Calexico, a number of workers staked their own position in the debate. Raul Rubio said he's never seen a labor shortage in the 22 years he's worked in Imperial Valley and Yuma fields, and he's not seeing one now. "They're lying," he said, shaking a finger to make his point.
"The wages wouldn't be so low if there weren't enough workers." o o o The strongest argument for a new guest-worker program is its promise of changing the illicit system that has grown up over the past five decades. Illegal immigrants make up more than half of the current U.S. crop work force of 1.8 million, creating an apartheid underclass that has woven itself into the U.S. agricultural industry. Every year, 150,000 immigrants enter illegally, knowing that if they can make it across the border they can find work, often in the fields.
Each year, 300 to 400 people die of exposure, dehydration or abusive treatment during the journey. Once in the United States, many are reluctant to return home because of high smugglers' fees and tight border security.
They live furtive existences, ever vigilant to detection. Some 50,000 farm workers come to the United States legally each year through a small existing agricultural guest-worker program called H-2A. But growers complain that H-2A requires cumbersome proof that workers are needed, then can't deliver them in time to do the work.
They'd prefer a program modeled after the H-1B guest-worker program that has allowed U.S. technology firms to import more than a half million high-tech workers since 1990. The growers' arguments are compelling: [] Foreign workers could come here safely, earn legal U.S. wages and be freed from a life of hiding and exploitation. [] The influx of undocumented workers would slow, saving the U.S. government much of the $1 billion spent annually on border control. [] Employers could stop pretending they don't hire illegals.
And a blow would be dealt to counterfeiters who sell false documents to field workers. Such arguments have gained the growers unusual allies, including the United Farm Workers union.
Its president, Arturo Rodriguez, said the union and the growers have spent years coming to terms on the subject. "I tend to disagree with the industry that there's a real need and a real shortage of workers," Rodriguez said.
"But we have been clear that the only way we'll accept a guest-worker program is if workers' wages are protected, that housing is protected, that some type of transportation is protected and also that if there is a problem they can have somewhere to go for help." But guest-worker programs, wherever they have been implemented, often fall short of the lofty ideas that preceded them. The previous U.S. experience with such an arrangement -- the controversial "bracero" program that lasted from the end of World War II to the mid-1960s -- was rife with abuse and fraud. And when the bracero workers told friends and family about their U.S. earnings, Mexicans without documents also began coming to the United States, establishing an ongoing pattern of illegal migration. "No program like this could ever be implemented without triggering a new massive wave of immigration," said Dan Stein, director of the Federation for American Immigration Reform, or FAIR.
"The growers want cheap labor, but society ends up with needy people." Other countries also have found that guest workers become permanent residents. Of the Turks recruited to work in Germany from the 1950s to 1970s, about half stayed on. And 15 percent of the approximately 15,000 Mexicans recruited each year in a Canadian guest-worker program fail to return home.
Among those who participate five or more years, the number climbs to 50 percent. Guest-worker programs can also restrict individual freedoms. "The worker is dependent on the employer to get a visa for the next year," said Bruce Goldstein, co-director of the Farmworker Justice Fund. "This puts them in a very vulnerable economic status (that) prevents many from asking for wage increases or challenging unfair employment practices. It also denies workers the democratic freedoms this country was based on." o o o Two guest-worker measures are taking shape. One is being prepared by agricultural employers, with the backing of the United Farm Workers and some immigration advocacy groups. The other is being developed by Republican Sen. Phil Gramm of Texas, who wrote the H-1B high-tech measure, but helped kill last year's farm guest-worker bill. Gramm and four other U.S. senators traveled to Mexico City last month to discuss their ideas with President Fox. Both plans would authorize temporary use of foreigner workers in the United States.
They differ greatly, however, on recruitment, wages and housing, and on what Mexico's role should be. Some of the growers want to tap workers from countries other than Mexico. "I'd probably skip to El Salvador because they are great people and hard workers," said Scaroni, the Imperial Valley contractor. The growers also would give guest workers the right to someday live permanently in the United States.
Under Gramm's plan, they could not. The most controversial element of Gramm's plan offers guest-worker status first to undocumented immigrants already in the United States.
When the visas they would be granted expired, however, they'd have to go home and reapply to return. "The intent would be to assure employers weren't turning away Americans," said Gramm's press secretary, Larry Neal.
"It will not be a revival of the old bracero program, nor will it be an amnesty program that invites people to come to stay." Last year's bill, Neal said, "was something like serfdom and it ended with amnesty.
And Senator Gramm is opposed to both serfdom and amnesty." To agricultural employers, the most important issue in any new legislation is who would determine when workers are needed -- who, in effect, can open the gate. Under the growers' proposal, they would merely notify the government that they need field hands.
That way, they say, they could streamline the process and get workers in time for harvests so crops wouldn't rot in the fields. The growers strongly oppose Gramm's suggestion that employers be required to exhaust local unemployment rolls before hiring guest workers. Vice, who contends that few people on unemployment are qualified for field work, said, "Under that system, we'd have a hard time getting any workers at all." o o o Imperial County is rare among U.S. agricultural regions in that its growers already have access to a huge group of foreign workers who cross the border legally each day from Mexicali. At the peak of the 3 a.m. to 5 a.m. commute, as many as 5,000 Mexicans walk through the pedestrian gates each hour.
They have U.S.-issued permits, which allow them to work days in the United States and go home to Mexico at night. But even with this abundance of workers, Imperial Valley growers are leading the call for more foreign workers. Agricultural experts are split on whether a guest-worker program would resolve this paradox.
They agree, however, that the likelihood of Congress passing a new guest-worker program has never been stronger. "What we have that is different is a president in Mexico who recognizes the problem in Mexico and wants to fix it," said Gramm spokesman Neal.
"And fortunately we have a president in Washington who also recognizes the problem and wants to fix it."

Science education and filling high-skilled jobs
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 29, 2000, Friday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION;Pg. B-7:2,7; B-13:1; OP ED COLUMN. STATISTICS.
Length: 961 words
Byline: William H. Rastetter; Rastetter is chairman, chief executive officer and president of IDEC Pharmaceuticals in San Diego.
Body

Suzanne Hunt has an ambition.  She wants to work with patients who are undergoing clinical trials, which are experimental studies to treat or prevent disease.
There is, however, a catch: the IDEC clinical research assistant, who graduated a couple of years ago from UC Irvine with a bachelor's degree in biology, didn't have the specific skills or on-the-job experience required to work with this special group of patients.
Not to be deterred, Suzanne enrolled in a professional certificate program in Clinical Trials Design and Management at UCSD Extension.  There, she learned the clinical trials' process and obtained the knowledge and training she needs to move ahead in a rewarding career.
This is one small example of how communication and cooperation between educators and employers can convert specialized training and development needs into enhanced job skills.  Still, it's just the start.
Two ominous trends -- the growing demand for high-tech visas and the large proportion of non-college-bound high school graduates -- indicate we need to do a better job training and developing employees and educating students for high-skilled jobs in San Diego now and in the decade ahead.
In the regional biotech and high-tech industries high-skilled workers are in short supply and their scarcity is expected to increase.  Companies would prefer to hire local workers but are often forced to recruit job candidates from other states or foreign countries.
IDEC, which signed an agreement in September to develop a world-class manufacturing and research and development campus in Oceanside, will seek to hire qualified personnel locally for the new facility when construction is completed in the next three or four years.  But if recent experience is any indication, we may have to look abroad to fill key management positions.  Recently, we had to recruit new managers from as far away as Germany and Argentina, and IDEC is not alone with this problem.
"Currently, 10 percent of the 153,000 biotech employees in the United States are H-1B visa holders -- highly skilled foreign workers with temporary work visas," said Joseph Panetta, president of BIOCOM San Diego, the regional trade association for the biotech and biomedical industries.
In San Diego about 1,500 H-1B visa holders are employed and demand for these highly-skilled workers could increase 25 percent by 2001, Panetta said.
In recent years, H-1B has become a hotly debated issue in Congress.  In 1998 legislators tried to solve the labor shortage in the biotech and high-tech industries by increasing foreign visas to 115,000 individuals per year from 65,000 individuals.  But the number decreases to 107,500 for the 12 months ending in October and to 65,000 in 2001.
Seeking another fix, the biotech and high-tech industries are supporting S. 2045, a bill by Sen. Orrin Hatch, R-Utah, which would increase the number of H-1Bs to 200,000 a year through 2003.
While the issue of H-1B highlights a crucial problem, stop-gap legislation won't address the deep, underlying cause -- a large proportion of San Diegans don't graduate from college; and of those who do, not enough are pursuing science and engineering careers.  These disturbing trends confine many to second-class status in our New Economy.
In 1995 the Third International Mathematics and Science Study (TIMSS), the largest, most comprehensive, and most rigorous international study of schools and students ever conducted, showed that American students lagged behind their international counterparts at key grade levels.  In addition to unsettling results, TIMSS also revealed a promising finding.  When students are instructed by confident, well-trained teachers and participate in content-rich math and science curricula, they can outperform their high-achieving international rivals.
In San Diego we're beginning to see hopeful signs that educators, employers and elected officials are connecting math and science education with workplace readiness and regional competitiveness.  One hopeful sign is California's and the nation's first High Tech High School, which opened its doors on Sept. 18 to 200 ninth and 10th graders on the former Naval Training Cener in Point Loma. A tuition-free, publicly financed charter school open to all students, High Tech High provides project-based learning in math and science and real-world industry experience.
Another hopeful sign is the San Diego Workforce Partnership, which recognizes the critical shortage of skilled workers and is making efforts to curb it.  The partnership is coordinating a regional workforce-planning symposium called "San Diego's Workforce -- Our Competitive Edge," at the San Diego Convention Center today.  At this daylong, planning session, employers will discuss employee skill requirements and educators will try to translate those needs into curricula and training programs.
"The point of the conference is to begin to determine how best to develop a pipeline of trained workers who will meet businesses needs," said Lawrence Fitch, president and CEO of the Workforce Partnership.
There is no lack of creativity and excitement in our community.  You can see it in the launching of High Tech High, the formation of the San Diego Workforce Partnership, and the number and variety of professional certificate programs at UCSD and San Diego State University.
These efforts and future initiatives will help close the gap between skilled and unskilled workers in San Diego's biotech and high-tech industries.
Still, we must remember that high-skilled, high-salaried jobs are only part of what we seek.  The other part, the most important part, is creating exciting opportunities for talented, highly motivated teams of employees who -- despite what cynics say -- can and will change the world.

Industry: NAICS541720 RESEARCH & DEVELOPMENT IN THE SOCIAL SCIENCES & HUMANITIES  (85%);  NAICS325414 BIOLOGICAL PRODUCT (EXCEPT DIAGNOSTIC) MANUFACTURING  (85%);  NAICS325412 PHARMACEUTICAL PREPARATION MANUFACTURING  (85%); CLINICAL TRIALS (90%); PHARMACEUTICALS PRODUCT DEVELOPMENT (90%); EMPLOYMENT VISAS (89%); FACTORY WORKERS (89%); PASSPORTS & VISAS (89%); PHARMACEUTICALS & BIOTECHNOLOGY (89%); BIOMEDICINE (78%); HIGH SCHOOLS (78%); MANUFACTURING (78%); NEW CONSTRUCTION (72%); SECONDARY SCHOOLS (54%)

Geographic: SAN DIEGO, CA, USA (94%); CALIFORNIA, USA (92%); GERMANY (79%); UNITED STATES (79%)

Growing issue; Farmers see a labor crunch, field hands don't, as chances of guest-worker legislation improve
The San Diego Union-Tribune
February 23, 2001, Friday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: NEWS;Pg. A-1; STATISTICS.
Length: 1935 words
Byline: Diane Lindquist; STAFF WRITER
Body

CALEXICO -- By 4:30 a.m. most mornings, as many as 10,000 people are huddled on the street corners and in the parking lots of this small downtown, hoping to be hired for work in the fields.
But when the employers' vans and buses pull away, scores of men and women are routinely left behind -- another day without work.
This scene at the gateway to the Imperial Valley, America's winter salad bowl, happened time and again in January.  Workers far exceeded jobs, just as they routinely do in fruit- and vegetable-growing regions throughout the United States.  It's an impression bolstered by high unemployment rates in farming regions, with Imperial County's among the highest at 23 percent.
Across the country in the nation's capital, however, a contradictory story is told.
Farm owners are complaining to Congress of a serious labor shortage.  The only solution, they say, is a new guest-worker program that would allow them to temporarily hire foreigners.
But if growers can't find enough people to harvest their fields, why are unemployment rates so high in agricultural communities?  Why is there a labor shortage amid a labor surplus?
Conflicting answers to these questions have been offered for decades, as guest-worker legislation has been introduced in Congress -- and voted down.
This year, however, guest-worker legislation is gaining new momentum.
When President Bush and Mexican President Vicente Fox met this month, they formed a high-level team charged, among other things, with producing a guest-worker plan.
Both presidents believe that such a program would help stem illegal immigration and protect Mexican workers in the United States.
"The sense I've got is, this is the year if we can put the right plan together," said Bob Vice, a Fallbrook grower who as president of the National Council of Agricultural Employers leads the lobbying effort.  The organization represents three-quarters of the country's farm employers.
Imperial County contract harvester Steve Scaroni points to his own situation as an example of why a guest-worker program is needed.
Every day, his buses load up workers in a Calexico parking lot.  And even though thousands of people are there looking for work, he says that in the past two months his crews have been shorthanded by 5 to 50 percent.
Scaroni offered explanations for why there appear to be more workers than jobs most mornings.
"They probably don't want to work in the first place.  Or they're very selective about what jobs they'll do," he said.  "A lot come for the socializing, to see their friends."
Scaroni also notes that field jobs, which pay slightly more or less than California's minimum wage of $6.25 an hour, aren't as attractive as other entry-level jobs, like construction.
Picking fruit and vegetables is physically draining, and people quickly switch to better-paying, year-round positions.
But there's little hard evidence to back up the growers' claim of a labor shortage.
The California Employment Development Department "doesn't have anything that documents a labor shortage" in farm communities, said an agency spokeswoman.
In fact, some of the state's highest unemployment rates in December were in agricultural regions -- the 23 percent in Imperial County, 14 percent in Fresno and Madera counties, 12 percent in Monterey County, 11 percent in Kern County, 9 percent in San Joaquin County.
The national unemployment rate, meanwhile, was 4 percent.
After the harvest, the rates soar even higher in agricultural regions.  In July, Imperial County's rate was 34 percent.
The Employment Development Department's director, Michael Bernick, points out that many of the people collecting unemployment wouldn't be suited for field work.
But he also said that California's agricultural community tends to bypass the state employment system, turning instead to informal hiring networks like the morning gatherings in downtown Calexico.
Even Fallbrook grower Vice admits there's no proof of labor shortages.
"But it's been tight," he adds quickly, noting that employers are going to great lengths to fill their crews.  Some offer incentives, such as paying part of the wages up front, he said.  Others accept less-qualified applicants, including those who seem drunk.
Outside the Donut Avenue cafe in Calexico, a number of workers staked their own position in the debate.
Raul Rubio said he's never seen a labor shortage in the 22 years he's worked in Imperial Valley and Yuma fields, and he's not seeing one now.
"They're lying," he said, shaking a finger to make his point.  "The wages wouldn't be so low if there weren't enough workers."
o o o
The strongest argument for a new guest-worker program is its promise of changing the illicit system that has grown up over the past five decades.
Illegal immigrants make up more than half of the current U.S. crop work force of 1.8 million, creating an apartheid underclass that has woven itself into the U.S. agricultural industry.
Every year, 150,000 immigrants enter illegally, knowing that if they can make it across the border they can find work, often in the fields.  Each year, 300 to 400 people die of exposure, dehydration or abusive treatment during the journey.
Once in the United States, many are reluctant to return home because of high smugglers' fees and tight border security.  They live furtive existences, ever vigilant to detection.
Some 50,000 farm workers come to the United States legally each year through a small existing agricultural guest-worker program called H-2A.
But growers complain that H-2A requires cumbersome proof that workers are needed, then can't deliver them in time to do the work.  They'd prefer a program modeled after the H-1B guest-worker program that has allowed U.S. technology firms to import more than a half million high-tech workers since 1990.
The growers' arguments are compelling:
[] Foreign workers could come here safely, earn legal U.S. wages and be freed from a life of hiding and exploitation.
[] The influx of undocumented workers would slow, saving the U.S. government much of the $1 billion spent annually on border control.
[] Employers could stop pretending they don't hire illegals.  And a blow would be dealt to counterfeiters who sell false documents to field workers.
Such arguments have gained the growers unusual allies, including the United Farm Workers union.  Its president, Arturo Rodriguez, said the union and the growers have spent years coming to terms on the subject.
"I tend to disagree with the industry that there's a real need and a real shortage of workers," Rodriguez said.  "But we have been clear that the only way we'll accept a guest-worker program is if workers' wages are protected, that housing is protected, that some type of transportation is protected and also that if there is a problem they can have somewhere to go for help."
But guest-worker programs, wherever they have been implemented, often fall short of the lofty ideas that preceded them.
The previous U.S. experience with such an arrangement -- the controversial "bracero" program that lasted from the end of World War II to the mid-1960s -- was rife with abuse and fraud.
And when the bracero workers told friends and family about their U.S. earnings, Mexicans without documents also began coming to the United States, establishing an ongoing pattern of illegal migration.
"No program like this could ever be implemented without triggering a new massive wave of immigration," said Dan Stein, director of the Federation for American Immigration Reform, or FAIR.  "The growers want cheap labor, but society ends up with needy people."
Other countries also have found that guest workers become permanent residents.
Of the Turks recruited to work in Germany from the 1950s to 1970s, about half stayed on.
And 15 percent of the approximately 15,000 Mexicans recruited each year in a Canadian guest-worker program fail to return home.  Among those who participate five or more years, the number climbs to 50 percent.
Guest-worker programs can also restrict individual freedoms.
"The worker is dependent on the employer to get a visa for the next year," said Bruce Goldstein, co-director of the Farmworker Justice Fund.
"This puts them in a very vulnerable economic status (that) prevents many from asking for wage increases or challenging unfair employment practices.  It also denies workers the democratic freedoms this country was based on."
o o o
Two guest-worker measures are taking shape.
One is being prepared by agricultural employers, with the backing of the United Farm Workers and some immigration advocacy groups.
The other is being developed by Republican Sen. Phil Gramm of Texas, who wrote the H-1B high-tech measure, but helped kill last year's farm guest-worker bill.
Gramm and four other U.S. senators traveled to Mexico City last month to discuss their ideas with President Fox.
Both plans would authorize temporary use of foreigner workers in the United States.  They differ greatly, however, on recruitment, wages and housing, and on what Mexico's role should be.
Some of the growers want to tap workers from countries other than Mexico.
"I'd probably skip to El Salvador because they are great people and hard workers," said Scaroni, the Imperial Valley contractor.
The growers also would give guest workers the right to someday live permanently in the United States.  Under Gramm's plan, they could not.
The most controversial element of Gramm's plan offers guest-worker status first to undocumented immigrants already in the United States.  When the visas they would be granted expired, however, they'd have to go home and reapply to return.
"The intent would be to assure employers weren't turning away Americans," said Gramm's press secretary, Larry Neal.  "It will not be a revival of the old bracero program, nor will it be an amnesty program that invites people to come to stay."
Last year's bill, Neal said, "was something like serfdom and it ended with amnesty.  And Senator Gramm is opposed to both serfdom and amnesty."
To agricultural employers, the most important issue in any new legislation is who would determine when workers are needed -- who, in effect, can open the gate.
Under the growers' proposal, they would merely notify the government that they need field hands.  That way, they say, they could streamline the process and get workers in time for harvests so crops wouldn't rot in the fields.
The growers strongly oppose Gramm's suggestion that employers be required to exhaust local unemployment rolls before hiring guest workers.
Vice, who contends that few people on unemployment are qualified for field work, said, "Under that system, we'd have a hard time getting any workers at all."
o o o
Imperial County is rare among U.S. agricultural regions in that its growers already have access to a huge group of foreign workers who cross the border legally each day from Mexicali.
At the peak of the 3 a.m. to 5 a.m. commute, as many as 5,000 Mexicans walk through the pedestrian gates each hour.  They have U.S.-issued permits, which allow them to work days in the United States and go home to Mexico at night.
But even with this abundance of workers, Imperial Valley growers are leading the call for more foreign workers.
Agricultural experts are split on whether a guest-worker program would resolve this paradox.  They agree, however, that the likelihood of Congress passing a new guest-worker program has never been stronger.
"What we have that is different is a president in Mexico who recognizes the problem in Mexico and wants to fix it," said Gramm spokesman Neal.  "And fortunately we have a president in Washington who also recognizes the problem and wants to fix it."

Science education and filling high-skilled jobs
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 29, 2000 Friday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. B-7:2,7; B-13:1
Length: 958 words
Byline: William H. Rastetter, Rastetter is chairman, chief executive officer and president of IDEC Pharmaceuticals in San Diego.
Body

In recent years, H-1B has become a hotly debated issue in Congress.
In 1998 legislators tried to solve the labor shortage in the biotech and high-tech industries by increasing foreign visas to 115,000 individuals per year from 65,000 individuals.
But the number decreases to 107,500 for the 12 months ending in October and to 65,000 in 2001. Seeking another fix, the biotech and high-tech industries are supporting S. 2045, a bill by Sen. Orrin Hatch, R-Utah, which would increase the number of H-1Bs to 200,000 a year through 2003. While the issue of H-1B highlights a crucial problem, stop-gap legislation won't address the deep, underlying cause -- a large proportion of San Diegans don't graduate from college; and of those who do, not enough are pursuing science and engineering careers.
These disturbing trends confine many to second-class status in our New Economy. In 1995 the Third International Mathematics and Science Study (TIMSS), the largest, most comprehensive, and most rigorous international study of schools and students ever conducted, showed that American students lagged behind their international counterparts at key grade levels.
In addition to unsettling results, TIMSS also revealed a promising finding.
When students are instructed by confident, well-trained teachers and participate in content-rich math and science curricula, they can outperform their high-achieving international rivals. In San Diego we're beginning to see hopeful signs that educators, employers and elected officials are connecting math and science education with workplace readiness and regional competitiveness.
One hopeful sign is California's and the nation's first High Tech High School, which opened its doors on Sept. 18 to 200 ninth and 10th graders on the former Naval Training Cener in Point Loma. A tuition-free, publicly financed charter school open to all students, High Tech High provides project-based learning in math and science and real-world industry experience. Another hopeful sign is the San Diego Workforce Partnership, which recognizes the critical shortage of skilled workers and is making efforts to curb it.
The partnership is coordinating a regional workforce-planning symposium called "San Diego's Workforce -- Our Competitive Edge," at the San Diego Convention Center today.
At this daylong, planning session, employers will discuss employee skill requirements and educators will try to translate those needs into curricula and training programs. "The point of the conference is to begin to determine how best to develop a pipeline of trained workers who will meet businesses needs," said Lawrence Fitch, president and CEO of the Workforce Partnership. There is no lack of creativity and excitement in our community.
You can see it in the launching of High Tech High, the formation of the San Diego Workforce Partnership, and the number and variety of professional certificate programs at UCSD and San Diego State University. These efforts and future initiatives will help close the gap between skilled and unskilled workers in San Diego's biotech and high-tech industries. Still, we must remember that high-skilled, high-salaried jobs are only part of what we seek.
The other part, the most important part, is creating exciting opportunities for talented, highly motivated teams of employees who -- despite what cynics say -- can and will change the world.

South Asians searching cyber-world for spouses
The San Diego Union-Tribune
March 6, 2001, Tuesday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: COMPUTER LINK;Pg. 9
Length: 1422 words
Byline: Sarah Lubman; KNIGHT RIDDER NEWS SERVICE
Body

From a distance, she seemed perfect.  Her e-mail messages were witty, evoking the chemistry Tariq Ahmed longed for in a soul mate.
But after an 11-hour drive to Toledo, Ohio, Ahmed's vision of the perfect bride vaporized the moment they met.
"There was no mutual attraction," says Ahmed, a 29-year-old Web engineer in Palo Alto.  "It was like going to the counter and ordering something."
Ahmed, a Canadian citizen who is half Pakistani and half Austrian, wound up driving from Ottawa to Ohio because he wanted to find a Muslim wife without enduring the awkward ritual of family-brokered introductions.
So the self-described "Net freak" took the route favored by growing numbers of South Asians these days: He posted a personal ad on a matrimonial Web site geared specifically toward his culture, in which arranged marriage is still common.
Unlike broader matchmaking sites that hook up singles on dates, South Asian matrimonial sites, as the phrase implies, are frankly aimed at finding partners for life, wherever they are.
Geography is less of an obstacle than the uniquely South Asian categories of religion and caste, common search fields on matrimonial sites.
Ahmed pursued love through Matrimonials.com, run by 4You Net Services Inc. of Lakewood, Ohio.
Its site offers one link for Indians -- "for people of South Asian heritage" -- and another for Muslims "of any origin," most of whom are from the subcontinent.
The site charges a $30 membership fee to post a six-month ad that enters a searchable database of thousands of marriage-minded men and women of Indian and South Asian descent.
Modern technology
It's just one of scores of such Internet venues that have sprung up in recent years to accommodate sophisticated South Asians around the world, blending ethnic tradition with modern technology.
The sites are a high-tech version of a decades-old Indian custom of newspaper matrimonial ads, and a natural extension of a culture of matchmaking, whether between families, friends or professional marriage brokers.
Some young South Asians are turned off by the sites.
"The Web sites I see have guys on them like the ones my parents want me to marry, a.k.a. nerds," says Monica Bhatnagar, 24, who grew up in Alameda.  "They may be perfectly great husbands, but I prefer to find someone on my own."
That was harder for Ahmed.  The son of a religious Pakistani father and an Austrian mother who converted to Islam, he couldn't date at all.
"Tradition won't let you meet a person 50 times," he says.  "You have only two or three times, and then you make a decision."
'Nothing to say'
Family introductions in Ottawa were excruciating ordeals during which Ahmed and his potential bride "were just sitting there with nothing to say because parents are talking about things that happened in 1946," the tumultuous year that led to the partition of India and Pakistan.
Ahmed had another strategy to meet women.
"Weddings are your best bet," he says.  "I made sure I was at every wedding, dressed to the max."
But Ahmed's fashion displays didn't get results, and it didn't take him long to reject the handful of eligible Muslim women introduced by relatives.
"You go through everybody's list of contacts real fast," he says.  "My parents, uncles and seven aunts could only come up with a handful of daughters.  I decided, OK, the only way this is going to happen is if I have a lot more control."
When Ahmed turned 24 in 1996, he decided to conduct his own search.
A Web ad seemed an efficient and more individualistic way to find a spouse than the hidebound methods of family setups, newspaper ads and paid matchmakers.
"My parents had no idea what was going on," he says.  "It was stealth."
Ahmed scanned other matrimonial ads and strove to outdo them with his computer engineering background.
The result was a graphically sophisticated ad featuring "husband model 49321." It included pictures of Ahmed's cat and statistics on his lung capacity -- 2 liters -- in order to stand out amid the crowded databases of eligible South Asian singles.
Ahmed's sassy ad subtly mocked the usual sought-after attributes such as skin color.  Fair skin, or "wheatish" as they say in India, is highly valued among South Asians, and some matrimonial Web sites include it as a search category.
The largest sites -- such as Matrimonials.com, IndianMarriages.com, SuitableMatch.com, Matrimonial- online.com and Cyberproposal.com -- all allow members to seek dream spouses with categories ranging from simple criteria such as height, caste and horoscope to ultra-detailed scans tailor-made for the South Asian diaspora.
If you're in the market for, say, a fair-skinned, vegetarian, Sikh engineer in the United States on an H-1B visa, IndianMarriages.com's site can look for his ad.
But matrimonial-site operators say the most requested search categories are weight, height, religion, caste and occupation.
Hundreds of responses
Ahmed got more than 400 e-mail responses from all over the world.  He wrote back to each and every one.
He began spending eight to 12 hours a day -- at work -- sending e-mail, whittling down the list and eventually driving to meet 17 women in places as far as Toledo and Milwaukee.
"I only have so much vacation time, and I have to drive," Ahmed says.  "I can't spend thousands of dollars on flights to meet someone when statistically the odds that they're the one are low."
His choices were far more numerous today than they would have been even a decade ago.
The number of expatriate South Asians has swelled, propelled in part by a technology boom that has lured software-proficient programmers and engineers to American and Canadian soil.
By 1997, there were 748,000 Indian-born U.S. residents, up 66 percent from 1990. As the ranks of South Asians have grown, matchmaking sites to serve them have evolved well beyond their early prototypes.
Now the sites are round-the-clock businesses, and some say they are profitable, though they won't disclose numbers.
Matrimonialonline.com, one of the largest sites, operates out of the Fremont apartment of Suresh Ganapathy, whose day job is as an information technology manager at a financial services firm in San Francisco.
Ganapathy maintains the 5-year-old site with the help of an office in India and his family.  His sister, Subbulakshmi, is a software engineer who backs up the database after she gets home from her day job in Cupertino.
Ganapathy's wife, Priya Suresh, works full time answering e-mail and modifying profiles of their 2,800 paid members.
The family spends weekends tending to the site, which generates an average of 300 e-mails a day.
The earliest paid members were mostly South Asian men living in the United States and Canada.  But now membership is more evenly split between the genders, with more and more men and women posting ads from India.  The same holds true for other sites.
Gautam Ghosh, an anthropologist at the University of Pennsylvania, finds symbolism in the use of the Internet to find a South Asian spouse, given growing pride in India's role as a software superpower.
"People think the software industry is what will get India out of its Third World stigma," Ghosh says.  "The medium is the message here.  The very fact that you're using an online service suggests you're desirable."
The medium took a few years to pay off for Ahmed.
After two years of advertising himself on the Net, Ahmed was disappointed -- and exhausted.  His Toledo rendezvous was the first of many, and they went downhill from there.
"My e-mails were getting shorter and shorter," he says.  "Sometimes I'd just cut and paste my lifelong story."
In 1998, Ahmed moved to the Bay Area on a special work visa.  He took a break from his search, but kept exchanging e-mail with a woman in Singapore named Juliana Gidwani.
Like Ahmed, Gidwani is racially mixed -- half Indonesian, half Indian -- and had similar interests.
One day, Ahmed says, they were "talking ICQ" -- chatting in real time on the Net -- when something clicked.
Ahmed rolled the dice.  He'd seen Gidwani's picture and thought she was attractive, so he bought a ticket to Singapore, regarding it as a vacation if nothing panned out.
Cupid struck at the arrival gate.
"As soon as I saw her at the airport, I knew she was the one," Ahmed says.  He asked for her hand two days later.
Three months after that, in September 1999, they were married in an elaborate three-day celebration in Singapore, which he posted to the Web (http://www.dopejam.com/memories/mywed). 
They live in Tracy so Gidwani can commute to an MBA program at the University of California Davis.

Vote near to allow more high-tech worker visas
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 30, 2000 Saturday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS; Pg. C-1
Length: 646 words
Byline: Joe Cantlupe, COPLEY NEWS SERVICE
Dateline: WASHINGTON 
Body


WASHINGTON -- The Senate is poised to pass a bill next week to increase the number of immigrant high-tech workers -- a step sought by San Diego's Qualcomm and technology companies in Silicon Valley -- following intense political maneuvering on Capitol Hill. But there's more politicking to come, both Democrats and Republicans predict. After months of delay, the so-called H-1B visa bill hit more snags this past week as Republican leaders blocked Democrats' attempts to attach amendments that would grant amnesty to some long-term illegal residents and to refugees from Central America and the Caribbean. While some Republicans termed the amnesty package simple pandering for votes, Latinos seeking the measures blasted the GOP for lacking the "compassionate conservatism" embraced by their presidential contender, Texas Gov. 
George W. Bush. Now, both the amnesty and the high-tech measures seem to be going their separate ways. Democrats vowed to attach the immigration reforms to appropriation bills. Democratic leaders also insisted they would sustain a threatened White House veto if the amnesty measures are not included. The maneuvering highlighted a congressional session that is winding down while a national election campaign is heating up. Both parties are trying to tap the growing influence of the Latino electorate, as well as the clout of the high-tech industry, a heavy contributor to political campaigns. The high-tech visa bill seems certain of passage.
The Senate anticipates considering the legislation -- without the amnesty amendments -- as early as Tuesday. Many technology companies like Qualcomm and others in San Diego that have long sought to increase the number of high-tech visas say hundreds of thousands of jobs are going unfilled nationwide because of the lack of qualified workers. Delays in obtaining more visas have resulted in "lost sales, stalled or shelved projects," said Sandra J. Boyd, chairwoman of the American Business for Legal Immigration, a coalition of high-tech businesses. Despite the high-tech industry's political clout, some labor groups contend that older American workers have been shortchanged by the high-tech visa program because it has lacked protections for U.S. employees.
Other problems include allegations of fraud and lack of oversight by the Immigration and Naturalization Service, according to congressional investigators. Under current law, the government issued 115,000 of the visas this year for skilled workers from abroad, most of whom are from India.
But that ceiling falls to 107,500 for the next fiscal year, which begins tomorrow, and to 65,000 in the fiscal year after that.
Industry officials say they already have exhausted the existing supply of visas, which expire after six years. The new measure would grant about 195,000 high-tech visas in each of the next three years. "Clearly," said Senate Minority Leader Tom Daschle, D-S.D., "there is strong bipartisan support in the Senate for increasing the number of visas for high-skilled workers." The amnesty bill package has a far less certain future. Some key Senate Republicans echoed the sentiments of Sen. Judd Gregg, R-N.H., who criticized the Clinton administration for trying to "create a new class of citizens independent of what is present law." But Latino leaders said they have obtained some Republican support that makes them hopeful for the legislation's passage. One measure would grant amnesty for 500,000 immigrants who entered the United States illegally, but have lived in this country since before 1986. Another would offer permanent residence to political refugees from El Salvador, Guatemala, Honduras and Haiti similar to those granted to people who fled Cuba and Nicaragua. "These people deserve fair treatment," said Sen. Dick Durbin, D-Ill.
"These families are building lives and need a chance to be treated fairly under the law."

President's plan rewards immigrants for illegal entry
The San Diego Union-Tribune
July 27, 2001 Friday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION; Pg. B-7
Length: 739 words
Byline: Joseph Perkins, THE SAN DIEGO UNION-TRIBUNE
Body


Louisa Luk arrived in San Diego by way of an F-1 student visa.
As a condition of being allowed in this country, the Hong Kong native had to not only prove she had the financial means to pay for her American education but also substantiate her "nonimmigrant" intent. In other words, she had to guarantee U.S. immigration officials that, upon completion of her studies, she would return to her home country.
If she wanted to stay here and work -- too bad. She has to go back home and hope she's fortunate enough to score one of the limited number of H-1B visas allotted each year by the Immigration and Naturalization Service. Which is why Luk is offended that the Bush administration is considering a plan to bestow permanent legal status -- don't call it "amnesty," the White House implores -- upon many of the 3 million Mexicans living illegally in the United States. The idea is to first grant Mexican illegals temporary "guest worker" legal status.
Then the freshly minted Mexican guest workers could apply for permanent legal status. That's all well and good as far as Luk is concerned.
But she wonders why the Bush administration plan, developed by Secretary of State Colin Powell and Attorney General John Ashcroft, is not being equally magnanimous to other illegal immigrant populations. "I don't doubt that Mexicans are hardworking," she says.
"They do a lot of jobs that Americans don't want to do.
But so do the Chinese and other (immigrant) groups that are desperate to become legal in the `Land of Plenty'." Luk is right.
It is patently unfair to give illegal immigrants from Mexico preferential treatment over illegals hailing from other countries.
The 14th Amendment guarantees "equal protection of the laws."
Yet, the Bush administration proposes to give Mexican illegals more-than-equal protection. But even if the White House proposed to give all 8 million illegal immigrants in this country permanent legal status, it would still be wrongheaded immigration policy.
Because it would make a mockery of the principle, says Luk, that "everyone should have to come to the U.S. the legal way." Of course, to publicly state this view, particularly with respect to illegal Mexican immigrants, is to run the risk of being tarred as racist or xenophobic or both.
Of being an immigrant basher like former California Gov. Pete Wilson or former GOP presidential candidate Pat Buchanan. But the folks who tend to feel strongest about this are other immigrants, like Luk, who have followed the letter of U.S. immigration law; who have taken the time, trouble and expense to enter this country the right way. Indeed, Texas Sen. Phil Gramm noted last week that some 7 million Mexican immigrants have legally applied to enter the country and are waiting for INS to approve their paperwork. It seems an insult to these legal Mexican immigrants that 3 million of their less-scrupulous countrymen should be able to win permanent legal status without doing anything more than stealing across the border. If the Bush administration's proposal to "regularize" (remember, don't call it "amnesty") illegal Mexican immigrants would solve the illegal immigration problem once and for all -- or at least reduce the numbers of illegals besieging this country each year -- then it might be worth implementation. But it is hard to see how this 2001 plan to confer legal status on up to 3 million undocumented Mexicans will have any more positive effect on illegal immigration than the 1986 Immigration Reform and Control Act, which previously granted legal status to 3 million illegal immigrants. Indeed, the one indisputable outcome of legalizing illegal immigrants is to tacitly encourage even more illegal immigration.
For if federal lawmakers granted amnesty in 1986, if they approve "regularization" in 2001, there's no reason not to expect that another round of amnesty will come around, say, 2016. To oppose granting permanent legal status for Mexican illegal immigrants is not to oppose immigration altogether -- although amnesty advocates attempt to confuse the issues.
It is only to say that those who violate this nation's immigration laws should not be rewarded with amnesty. For the United States will never control its borders unless it sends a clear message to people around who desire to immigrate to this land of the free, home of the brave: Come one.
Come all.
But come legally. Perkins can be reached via e-mail at joseph.perkins@uniontrib.com.

Publication-Type: Newspaper

Industry: PASSPORTS & VISAS (92%); EMPLOYMENT VISAS (78%); ATTORNEYS GENERAL (74%)

Vote near to allow more high-tech worker visas
The San Diego Union-Tribune
September 30, 2000, Saturday


Copyright 2000 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. C-1; STATISTICS.
Length: 645 words
Byline: Joe Cantlupe; COPLEY NEWS SERVICE
Body

President's plan rewards immigrants for illegal entry
The San Diego Union-Tribune
July 27, 2001, Friday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION;Pg. B-7; OP ED COLUMN.
Length: 741 words
Byline: Joseph Perkins; THE SAN DIEGO UNION-TRIBUNE
Body

Louisa Luk arrived in San Diego by way of an F-1 student visa.  As a condition of being allowed in this country, the Hong Kong native had to not only prove she had the financial means to pay for her American education but also substantiate her "nonimmigrant" intent.
In other words, she had to guarantee U.S. immigration officials that, upon completion of her studies, she would return to her home country.  If she wanted to stay here and work -- too bad.
She has to go back home and hope she's fortunate enough to score one of the limited number of H-1B visas allotted each year by the Immigration and Naturalization Service.
Which is why Luk is offended that the Bush administration is considering a plan to bestow permanent legal status -- don't call it "amnesty," the White House implores -- upon many of the 3 million Mexicans living illegally in the United States.
The idea is to first grant Mexican illegals temporary "guest worker" legal status.  Then the freshly minted Mexican guest workers could apply for permanent legal status.
That's all well and good as far as Luk is concerned.  But she wonders why the Bush administration plan, developed by Secretary of State Colin Powell and Attorney General John Ashcroft, is not being equally magnanimous to other illegal immigrant populations.
"I don't doubt that Mexicans are hardworking," she says.  "They do a lot of jobs that Americans don't want to do.  But so do the Chinese and other (immigrant) groups that are desperate to become legal in the 'Land of Plenty'."
Luk is right.  It is patently unfair to give illegal immigrants from Mexico preferential treatment over illegals hailing from other countries.  The 14th Amendment guarantees "equal protection of the laws." Yet, the Bush administration proposes to give Mexican illegals more-than-equal protection.
But even if the White House proposed to give all 8 million illegal immigrants in this country permanent legal status, it would still be wrongheaded immigration policy.  Because it would make a mockery of the principle, says Luk, that "everyone should have to come to the U.S. the legal way."
Of course, to publicly state this view, particularly with respect to illegal Mexican immigrants, is to run the risk of being tarred as racist or xenophobic or both.  Of being an immigrant basher like former California Gov. Pete Wilson or former GOP presidential candidate Pat Buchanan.
But the folks who tend to feel strongest about this are other immigrants, like Luk, who have followed the letter of U.S. immigration law; who have taken the time, trouble and expense to enter this country the right way.
Indeed, Texas Sen. Phil Gramm noted last week that some 7 million Mexican immigrants have legally applied to enter the country and are waiting for INS to approve their paperwork.
It seems an insult to these legal Mexican immigrants that 3 million of their less-scrupulous countrymen should be able to win permanent legal status without doing anything more than stealing across the border.
If the Bush administration's proposal to "regularize" (remember, don't call it "amnesty") illegal Mexican immigrants would solve the illegal immigration problem once and for all -- or at least reduce the numbers of illegals besieging this country each year -- then it might be worth implementation.
But it is hard to see how this 2001 plan to confer legal status on up to 3 million undocumented Mexicans will have any more positive effect on illegal immigration than the 1986 Immigration Reform and Control Act, which previously granted legal status to 3 million illegal immigrants.
Indeed, the one indisputable outcome of legalizing illegal immigrants is to tacitly encourage even more illegal immigration.  For if federal lawmakers granted amnesty in 1986, if they approve "regularization" in 2001, there's no reason not to expect that another round of amnesty will come around, say, 2016.
To oppose granting permanent legal status for Mexican illegal immigrants is not to oppose immigration altogether -- although amnesty advocates attempt to confuse the issues.  It is only to say that those who violate this nation's immigration laws should not be rewarded with amnesty.
For the United States will never control its borders unless it sends a clear message to people around who desire to immigrate to this land of the free, home of the brave: Come one.  Come all.  But come legally.
Perkins can be reached via e-mail at joseph.perkins@uniontrib.com.

Biotech is one of few industries still hiring
The San Diego Union-Tribune
October 21, 2001 Sunday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS; Pg. H-1
Length: 860 words
Byline: Penni Crabtree, STAFF WRITER
Body


Though few industries can claim to be recession-proof, the biotechnology sector is shaping up to be more resistant to pink slips than most. The already teetering economy, hit hard by the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks, has pushed some struggling industries to the brink and beyond.
High-tech or low -- from the telecom to the travel industry -- many companies are laying off employees or imposing hiring freezes. Yet the local and national market for skilled biotech workers remains strong, and in select areas -- such as chemistry and bioinformatics -- there are more jobs than people to fill them, say industry experts. Biotech companies are posting job positions in newspapers and trade journals, mounting career fairs, and paying executive search firms to fill positions at a time when other industries are hunkering down. At BioSpace.com, a leading Web site for biotech industry news, job postings continue to flourish.
The San Francisco company hosts a popular online "career center" where U.S. biotech companies post an average of 2,500 job openings each month. In the past six months, through the growing economic malaise and terrorist attacks, those job postings have dropped off only 1 percent, said BioSpace spokeswoman Lisa Roth. "Biotech is one of the few industries still actively hiring," said Roth, whose Web site attracts 800,000 visitors and 4 million page views each month.
"Companies are still getting funded, research and development is strong." Cushioning the biotech industry from the larger downturn are factors that would make conservative investors shudder: most biotechs don't have a product or make a profit, and don't have a prayer of getting either for years. So financial realities that drive decisions in more traditional industries -- quarterly profits and losses -- aren't such a force with biotech.
In biotech, what matters is how much money is raised from private and public offerings, the rate at which it's spent, and how much longer it will take to get a product to market. And on those fronts, the biotechnology sector is relatively well positioned, industry observers agree.
Many biotechs are flush with cash from record financings and initial public offerings completed last year, and the number of drugs and medical products in late-stage development are at a peak. Still, there are warning signs that biotech may be headed for some financially humbling times, particularly among startups that rely on venture capital funding. Venture capital companies are investing less money in biotech firms this year, in part because their ability to cash out has dimmed with the public's aversion for initial public offerings. "There is smart money sitting out there looking for good business plans and management, and those companies will get money," said Tom Murphy, who heads the biotech unit of R.J. Watkins & Co., a San Diego executive recruitment company.
"But venture capital is more cautious and judicious in the deals that they do, and that will influence what young companies can do." Yet despite a slowdown in venture capital that will cramp many biotechs' plans, the sector is still capturing a larger percentage of overall venture capital investment, according to the National Venture Capital Association. The biotech industry raised $3 billion in venture capital last year, about 3 percent of the total $100 billion.
This year, the biotech industry is expected to raise about $2 billion, or 4 percent of the anticipated $50 billion in venture capital distributed. Meanwhile, biotech companies are expanding as corporate partnerships get forged and products are approved.
Locally, Isis Pharmaceuticals, which recently signed a $200 million deal with Eli Lilly, expects to hire as many as 100 new employees in the coming year. Both Invitrogen Corp. and Idec Pharmaceuticals are adding significantly to their work forces.
And several large pharmaceutical companies are expanding in the area, including Novartis, Pfizer and Elan Pharmaceuticals, which is hosting a hiring fair next month. "There's no indication that employment is down at all.
In fact, it's just the other way around," said Joseph Panetta, president of Biocom, the local biotech industry association that represents 216 San Diego County companies. In some crucial areas, the biotech industry may even face additional employee shortages in coming months.
Locally, about 6.5 percent of the biotech work force is imported -- foreign professionals with hard-to-find skills who enter the country on H-1B visas. With the recent terrorist attacks, the mood on Capital Hill has cooled toward some immigration programs.
According to a survey last week by Lawyers Weekly, H-1B and other visa applications that were routinely approved in the past may now face long delays or rejections. "We've had calls from some who say Biocom should be more cautious about supporting the H-1B visa program in the wake of the terrorist situation -- that we should support more of a home-grown constituency," concedes Panetta.
"It doesn't sound good anymore to talk about it (the H-1B program)." Penni Crabtree's e-mail address is penni.crabtree@uniontrib.com. Her phone number is (619) 293-1237.

Biotech is one of few industries still hiring
The San Diego Union-Tribune
October 21, 2001, Sunday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: BUSINESS;Pg. H-1
Length: 861 words

Body

Though few industries can claim to be recession-proof, the biotechnology sector is shaping up to be more resistant to pink slips than most.

With the recent terrorist attacks, the mood on Capital Hill has cooled toward some immigration programs.  According to a survey last week by Lawyers Weekly, H-1B and other visa applications that were routinely approved in the past may now face long delays or rejections.
"We've had calls from some who say Biocom should be more cautious about supporting the H-1B visa program in the wake of the terrorist situation -- that we should support more of a home-grown constituency," concedes Panetta.  "It doesn't sound good anymore to talk about it (the H-1B program)."

Foreign tech workers look north
Highly skilled visa holders discover, embrace easier immigration path in Canada
The San Diego Union Tribune
November 17, 2019 Sunday
Final Edition


Copyright 2019 The San Diego Union-Tribune All Rights Reserved
Section: LOCAL; B; Pg. 1
Length: 1091 words
Byline: Hafsa Fathima
Highlight: Vikram Rangnekar, formerly of Silicon Valley, started the website MovNorth, which helps explain the process of moving to Canada.Vikram Rangnekar
Body


Ask Puneet Anand, 35, how he's adjusting to a new life in Toronto, and he doesn't miss a beat.
"I'm still getting used to the cold," he said. Rapidly dropping temperatures in southern Ontario are a change for Anand, who called San Diego home not too long ago.
"I miss the weather there," he said. "You just can't beat it."
Moving away wasn't always the plan. A former hardware engineer at Qualcomm, Anand was employed in the United States on an H-1B visa - a temporary status given to highly skilled foreign guest workers. A majority of workers are hired as engineers or computer scientists, with large populations in California. 
"It was a great company, and they filed for my green card early," he said. "But then in a few years, reality struck."
Like other Indian nationals, he was caught in a backlog for permanent residency in the United States. Employment-based green cards are capped at a certain number for every country, resulting in longer lines for nationalities with bigger pools of applicants, like India and China.
"I couldn't do anything but wait," said Anand. The uncertainty of permanent status made it harder to plan a future in America, he added. He began looking for solutions, eventually finding one that lay north of the border.
In 2019, Anand migrated to Canada through a program called the Global Skills Strategy (GSS). Implemented by the Canadian government in 2017, it allows expedited processing for a work visa, usually around two weeks. 
Faster immigration options for highly skilled workers have been growing. The Express Entry system was created in 2015 to manage permanent resident applications for skilled workers, awarding the status to qualified applicants.
Four years ago, around 10,000 foreign nationals received permanent residency through the program. In 2018, that number was nine times higher.
Though no data tracks highly skilled foreign workers moving from the United States to Canada, Indians and Chinese nationals working in tech accounted for the highest number of express entry admissions in 2018.
Immigration experts at the Toronto branch of law firm Fragomen have seen a large number of Indian nationals applying for permanent residency in Canada, a path that eventually leads to citizenship in three years.
Fast processing for immigration status is an advantage to both employees unsure of their status in the United States and employers, who could retain skilled workers in their foreign branches, said David Crawford, a partner at Fragomen's Toronto office.
"There's much greater certainty on the outcome," said Crawford. "It helps your whole decision making."
Apart from the backlog, other issues make the H1-B a more uncertain prospect than previous years. Denial rates have been steadily climbing, rising from 6 percent in 2015 to 24 percent in 2019, according to data reported by Mother Jones. Delays in processing visas prompted concern from more than 20 U.S. senators, who addressed the issues in a letter to U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services  in May.
Shalaka Maind, 29, was a biomedical engineer in Carlsbad, working on a student visa valid for only three years.
"My company applied for my visa twice, but I didn't get picked in the lottery," said Maind, referring to a computer generated random selection process that awards work visas every year.
Her husband also faced some obstacles finding his own work visa. Eventually, they decided to apply through the express entry system and migrate to Canada.
Saying goodbye to San Diego didn't come easily, but Maind said they've found a sense of stability in Toronto.
"It's a growing country," she said. "With the tech sector coming up, there are a lot of opportunities. Looking at our short-term and long-term options, we decided to pick coming here."
A small, still growing community of former H-1B tech workers has begun to burgeon in Toronto and Vancouver, cities that host a lineup of companies that rival Silicon Valley. While Google, Netflix and Microsoft have a large presence, startups have also begun to break ground.
Vikram Rangnekar, 36, founded one of them. The lure of starting his own venture drew him to leave behind a life in Silicon Valley.
"On an H-1B, you're tied to the employer you work for," he said, adding that a specific visa for entrepreneurs didn't exist. "And I'd always pursued startups. I had some friends who had begun startups in Toronto and I reached out to them."
Rangnekar decided to leave for Canada more than two years ago, using his LinkedIn account to document the experience.
"I just started blogging on LinkedIn and that exploded," he said. "A lot of people were interested in how I'd done it."
Answering that curiosity resulted in his next startup, a digital platform called MovNorth. A community forum and also a space for employers to seek out talent, the site is geared toward acquainting tech workers with the process of moving to Canada. Hundreds of threads receive replies every day, discussing the initial process of moving to Canada and settling in.
Many are former H1-B workers looking to build a home up north. Newcomers ask for housing and dining recommendations, whether  local schools are  good and inquire whether  anyone in the area would like to meet up.
"People tell me they moved here and found friends on the website," said Rangnekar.
Anand still speaks wistfully of San Diego's beaches and hikes to Potato Chip Rock. His new home, however, has been as welcoming.
"There's a very diverse community," he said of Canada's new wave of Indian immigrants. "In Qualcomm, most of the people we knew were engineers, but when people come through express entry, they're in various fields. I've learned a lot from people here."
Despite some restrictions, the H-1B is still the most sought-out visa in the United States. Over 150,000 petitions are filed every year, predominantly by tech giants who want to ensure that Silicon Valley is kept at the forefront of the tech sector.
But a quieter trend continues to build on forums like Rangnekar's, growing as Canada pushes itself to expand immigration and talent in its labor markets.
Though clients who've left the U.S. do express a desire to return, Fragomen attorney Audrea Golding said there is concern among U.S. companies that retaining them on work visas may become more difficult. Foreign branches of their offices may be a viable option, she added.
"Companies are doing more to keep the overseas options as a part of their long-term retention planning," said Golding. "And if you're in Canada, it's minimizing the disruption." 
hafsa.fathima@sduniontribune.com

LETTERS
The San Diego Union-Tribune
October 24, 2001, Wednesday


Copyright 2001 The San Diego Union-Tribune
Section: OPINION;Pg. B-9; LETTER TO ED.
Length: 891 words
Body

Sane immigration policy is first step
Re: "Unruly guests" and "Border security" (Editorials, Oct. 16):
Your editorials indicate that 40,000 cars and up to 130,000 individuals cross the San Diego border daily.  You then state that illegal immigration could be controlled if only more money was spent, and imply that terrorism would be stopped as a result.
Yet of the 19 Sept. 11 hijackers, three overstayed their visas and no records were found concerning six others, implying that the remainder were here legally.  Even 19 is a very small percentage of the millions of foreign visitors.  The odds of the Immigration and Naturalization Service finding the right ones are infinitesimal, no matter how much money is spent.
The U.S. population is not homogeneous, and foreigners do not stand out as they would in China.  It might be possible to spot them if we implemented a national ID card (a good idea), but even that probably wouldn't work as papers can be forged and a minimum wage motel clerk couldn't tell a good ID from a bad one.
As one terrorist pointed out in a recent interview, the good guys need to be very good and very lucky all the time.  The terrorists need be lucky only once.  Spending more money to control illegal immigration is not the way to go. JOHN E. GAHAN Mira Mesa
While increased surveillance at our borders is a welcome change with many long-term benefits, it's clear that, in the short term, there are neither enough border officers nor appropriate scanning equipment to accommodate the number of people crossing the border.
The result has been onerous waits in lines for thousands of innocent people who want to cross the border for shopping or to work.  Businesses and families on both sides of the border are suffering economic hardships as a result.
While we are in this state of limited border resources, why not take a cue from the Europeans, and have separate lines for those border-crossers who are not citizens of a NAFTA country?  Non-NAFTA citizens could then be subjected to further, more detailed scrutiny.  That would focus our limited border resources on those individuals most likely to do us harm, while allowing the citizens of Mexico, Canada and the United States to move more freely across our borders for work and business. LESLIE ANDERSON Encinitas
If Mexicans are having trouble crossing the border to get to their jobs on time, perhaps these jobs could be filled by unemployed Americans. CRANE JOHNSON Jacumba
A country with millions of illegal immigrants and run-away legal immigration is either in the hands of a bunch of gutless politicos or the victim of a terrible border policy.  It's high time to rectify both. JIM HARRIS Coronado
No one in our government ever takes responsibility for mistakes, even if it costs 6,000 lives.
Energy Secretary and former Sen. Spencer Abraham is the man most responsible for gutting the 1996 immigration law designed to make it harder for terrorists to enter this country.  Abraham thought it was "bad for business" when INS tried to set up a visa entry/exit tracking system at our borders.  He delayed and finally watered down this measure.
He also was the point man for multinational corporations that wanted to increase H-1B visas, though it was later proven that there never was a high-tech worker shortage.  These workers never were given background checks and most never returned to their countries after their visas expired. LARRY BROWN Riverside
A badge can lead to abuse of power
Not everyone can handle being given the authority of a badge and uniform.  Many are prone to make up their own rules, and are likely to be overbearing.  This is a problem that must be guarded against, especially as our security efforts are being expanded so quickly.
Robert Peterson appears to have experienced an example of this abuse of authority by having his pocket knife confiscated by border security.
Though we must expect to be inconvenienced in some ways under the new circumstances, it must be controlled by laws, not the whims of individuals who are supposed to be protecting our rights. BOB BOUCHELL Chula Vista

U.S. turning away skilled workers it needs
The San Diego Union Tribune
November 20, 2019 Wednesday
Final Edition


Copyright 2019 The San Diego Union-Tribune All Rights Reserved
Section: LOCAL; B; Pg. 5
Length: 282 words
Body


Since 1990, the H-1B visa program has let companies temporarily employ foreign workers in occupations requiring specialized skills and a bachelor's degree - when they struggle to find a U.S. worker to do the job. The sensible program has faced intermittentcontroversies over the years because some companies used the visas to replace higher-paid Americans with lower-paid foreign workers. Some economists argue that H-1B visa holders help suppress wages in fields like computer science.
But with 700,000 tech jobs unfilled in the U.S., the value of and need for the program is difficult to dispute. This is why more than 60 U.S. business school deans and CEOs wrote a joint letter to the Wall Street Journal last month imploring President Donald Trump to expand the program and allow more than 85,000 H-1B visas to be issued a year.
Perhaps he'll listen. Perhaps not. The Trump administration not only doesn't grasp the value of H-1B visas, it is averse to them. A new study by the National Foundation for American Policy, based on government data, shows denial rates for H-1B visa requests have skyrocketed from 6% in 2015 to 24%. The White House also is seeking to make it more difficult for the spouses of H-1B visa holders to work in the U.S. No wonder that many foreigners working in the San Diego area are looking to relocate to Canada, a nation more welcoming of skilled immigrants.
Given that hostility to foreigners is the root of Trump's success as a politician, this is no surprise. Yet it is bad news for the U.S. economy - and for the future of a nation with an aging work force and a declining birthrate. The U.S. could use highly talented immigrants. The president should know this.
